## 1.
Описание проблем клиента — на картинке `project.png`.

## 2.
Файл `All_domains_process_v2.py`:
```
#!/usr/bin/env python3
import argparse
import json
import os
import time
from typing import Optional, Dict, List, Union
import logging
import re
import anthropic

# Set up logging
# File handler - detailed debug logging
file_handler = logging.FileHandler('analysis_debug.log')
file_handler.setLevel(logging.DEBUG)
file_handler.setFormatter(logging.Formatter('%(asctime)s - %(levelname)s - %(message)s'))

# Console handler - only important info
console_handler = logging.StreamHandler()
console_handler.setLevel(logging.INFO)
console_handler.setFormatter(logging.Formatter('%(message)s'))

# Set up logger
logger = logging.getLogger(__name__)
logger.setLevel(logging.DEBUG)
logger.addHandler(file_handler)
logger.addHandler(console_handler)

class DomainAnalyzer:
    def __init__(self, api_key: str):
        self.client = anthropic.Client(api_key=api_key)
        self.metamodel_data = {}
        # Ensure output directory exists
        os.makedirs("output", exist_ok=True)
        # Load existing metamodel if it exists
        self._load_existing_metamodel()

    def _load_existing_metamodel(self):
        """Load existing metamodel if it exists."""
        try:
            # Try to load from unknown first
            filename = os.path.join("output", "unknown_organization_metamodel.json")
            if os.path.exists(filename):
                with open(filename, 'r', encoding='utf-8') as f:
                    self.metamodel_data = json.load(f)
                logger.info(f"Loaded existing metamodel from {filename}")
        except Exception as e:
            logger.warning(f"Could not load existing metamodel: {str(e)}")

    def _get_metamodel_filename(self) -> str:
        """Get appropriate metamodel filename based on current data."""
        org_name = "unknown_organization"
        if "Organization" in self.metamodel_data:
            org_data = self.metamodel_data["Organization"]
            if isinstance(org_data, dict) and org_data.get("Title", "N/A") not in ["N/A", ""]:
                org_name = org_data["Title"].lower().replace(' ', '_')
                # Remove special characters except underscore
                org_name = re.sub(r'[^a-z0-9_]', '', org_name)
        
        return os.path.join("output", f"{org_name}_metamodel.json")

    def _chunk_document(self, content: str, max_chunk_size: int = 12000) -> list:
        """Split document into manageable chunks."""
        paragraphs = content.split('\n\n')
        chunks = []
        current_chunk = []
        current_size = 0

        for para in paragraphs:
            para_size = len(para)
            if current_size + para_size > max_chunk_size and current_chunk:
                chunks.append('\n\n'.join(current_chunk))
                current_chunk = []
                current_size = 0
            current_chunk.append(para)
            current_size += para_size

        if current_chunk:
            chunks.append('\n\n'.join(current_chunk))

        return chunks

    def _save_metamodel(self, metamodel: Dict):
        """Save current state of metamodel to file with better error handling."""
        try:
            # Update internal state
            self.metamodel_data.update(metamodel)
            
            # Get filename
            filename = self._get_metamodel_filename()
            
            # Create temp file first
            temp_filename = filename + '.tmp'
            with open(temp_filename, 'w', encoding='utf-8') as f:
                json.dump(self.metamodel_data, f, indent=2, ensure_ascii=False)
                f.write('\n')
            
            # Rename temp file to final file
            if os.path.exists(filename):
                os.replace(temp_filename, filename)
            else:
                os.rename(temp_filename, filename)
            
            logger.debug(f"Saved metamodel to {filename}")
            
        except Exception as e:
            logger.error(f"Error saving metamodel: {str(e)}")

    def _process_response(self, response_text: str) -> Union[Dict, List[Dict]]:
        """Process and validate response text into JSON."""
        try:
            # Clean up response text
            response_text = response_text.strip()
            
            # Extract JSON if wrapped in markdown
            if "```json" in response_text:
                response_text = response_text.split("```json")[1].split("```")[0].strip()
            elif "```" in response_text:
                response_text = response_text.split("```")[1].split("```")[0].strip()

            # Remove any "Here is a JSON..." prefix text
            if "{" in response_text:
                response_text = response_text[response_text.find("{"):]
            elif "[" in response_text:
                response_text = response_text[response_text.find("["):]
            
            # Parse JSON
            return json.loads(response_text)
            
        except json.JSONDecodeError as e:
            logger.error(f"JSON parsing error: {str(e)}")
            logger.error(f"Problematic response text: {response_text}")
            return {} if "[" not in response_text else []

    def _build_multi_instance_prompt(self, prompt: Dict, is_multi_instance: bool) -> str:
        """Build system prompt with awareness of multiple instances."""
        base_prompt = f"""You are analyzing an organizational document for {prompt['type']}.

Task: {prompt['task']}

Definition: {prompt['definition']['text']}

Extract information according to these attributes:
{json.dumps(prompt['extractionAndOutput']['attributes'], indent=2)}

Guidelines:
{json.dumps(prompt['guidelines'], indent=2)}"""

        if is_multi_instance:
            base_prompt += """

Special Instructions:
1. Return a LIST of JSON objects, one for each distinct instance found.
2. Each instance should contain all the specified attributes.
3. If no instances are found in this section, return an empty list [].
4. Avoid duplicating instances that refer to the same entity.
5. Preserve specific details for each instance."""
        else:
            base_prompt += """

Special Instructions:
1. Return a SINGLE JSON object with the specified attributes.
2. Use "N/A" for attributes where no information is found.
3. Be thorough in extracting information - look for both explicit and implicit mentions."""

        return base_prompt

    def _process_chunk_with_instances(
        self, 
        chunk: str, 
        prompt: Dict, 
        system_prompt: str,
        previous_results: Optional[Dict],
        is_multi_instance: bool
    ) -> Union[Dict, List[Dict]]:
        """Process a document chunk with support for multiple instances."""
        previous_context = ""
        if previous_results:
            previous_context = f"\n\nReference previous analysis if relevant:\n{json.dumps(previous_results, indent=2)}"

        user_message = f"""Analyze this document section and extract the requested information.{previous_context}

Document section:
{chunk}

Return {"a LIST of JSON objects for each instance found" if is_multi_instance else "a SINGLE JSON object"}."""

        max_retries = 3
        base_delay = 2
        
        for attempt in range(max_retries):
            try:
                response = self.client.messages.create(
                    model="claude-3-sonnet-20240229",
                    max_tokens=4096,
                    temperature=0,
                    system=system_prompt,
                    messages=[{"role": "user", "content": user_message}]
                )
                
                result = self._process_response(response.content[0].text)
                
                # Ensure result is in the correct format
                if is_multi_instance and not isinstance(result, list):
                    result = [result] if result else []
                elif not is_multi_instance and isinstance(result, list):
                    result = result[0] if result else {}
                    
                return result
                
            except anthropic.RateLimitError:
                if attempt < max_retries - 1:
                    delay = base_delay * (2 ** attempt)
                    logger.debug(f"Rate limit hit, waiting {delay} seconds...")
                    print(f"  Rate limit reached. Waiting {delay} seconds...", end="\r")
                    time.sleep(delay)
                    print("                                                ", end="\r")
                else:
                    raise

    def _merge_instances(self, existing_results: List[Dict], new_results: List[Dict]):
        """Merge new instances while avoiding duplicates."""
        for new_instance in new_results:
            # Skip empty or invalid instances
            if not new_instance or all(v == "N/A" for v in new_instance.values()):
                continue
                
            # Check if this instance is unique
            is_unique = True
            for existing in existing_results:
                if self._are_instances_similar(existing, new_instance):
                    # Merge any new information into existing instance
                    self._update_instance(existing, new_instance)
                    is_unique = False
                    break
                    
            if is_unique:
                existing_results.append(new_instance)

    def _are_instances_similar(self, instance1: Dict, instance2: Dict) -> bool:
        """Determine if two instances likely refer to the same entity."""
        # Compare title/name fields first if they exist
        title_fields = ['Title', 'Name', 'ID']
        for field in title_fields:
            if field in instance1 and field in instance2:
                if instance1[field] != "N/A" and instance2[field] != "N/A":
                    return instance1[field].lower() == instance2[field].lower()
        
        # Fall back to comparing significant fields
        significant_fields = set(instance1.keys()) & set(instance2.keys())
        matches = 0
        total_fields = 0
        
        for field in significant_fields:
            if instance1[field] != "N/A" and instance2[field] != "N/A":
                total_fields += 1
                if instance1[field] == instance2[field]:
                    matches += 1
        
        # Consider similar if high percentage of matching fields
        return total_fields > 0 and (matches / total_fields) > 0.8

    def _update_instance(self, existing: Dict, new_instance: Dict):
        """Update existing instance with new information."""
        for key, new_value in new_instance.items():
            if key in existing:
                if new_value != "N/A" and new_value != existing[key]:
                    if existing[key] == "N/A":
                        existing[key] = new_value
                    else:
                        # Combine unique information
                        existing[key] = '; '.join(set(filter(None, [
                            existing[key],
                            new_value
                        ])))

    def _merge_single_instance(self, existing_results: Dict, new_results: Dict):
        """Merge new results into existing single instance results."""
        for key, new_value in new_results.items():
            if key in existing_results:
                if new_value != "N/A" and new_value != existing_results[key]:
                    if existing_results[key] == "N/A":
                        existing_results[key] = new_value
                    else:
                        # Combine unique information
                        existing_results[key] = '; '.join(set(filter(None, [
                            existing_results[key],
                            new_value
                        ])))

    def analyze_domain(self, document_content: str, prompt: Dict, previous_results: Optional[Dict] = None) -> Dict:
        """Analyze document for a specific domain with support for multiple instances."""
        logger.debug(f"Processing domain: {prompt['type']}")
        print(f"\nAnalyzing {prompt['type']}...")
        
        # Check if this domain should collect multiple instances
        is_multi_instance = "instance" in prompt['extractionAndOutput']['instructions'].lower()
        
        if is_multi_instance:
            # Initialize as a list if we're collecting multiple instances
            domain_results = []
            
            # If we have existing results, start with those
            if prompt['type'] in self.metamodel_data:
                existing_data = self.metamodel_data[prompt['type']]
                if isinstance(existing_data, list):
                    domain_results = existing_data
                else:
                    # Convert existing single instance to list
                    domain_results = [existing_data] if any(v != "N/A" for v in existing_data.values()) else []
        else:
            # Single instance initialization
            domain_results = {attr: "N/A" for attr in prompt['extractionAndOutput']['attributes'].keys()}
            if prompt['type'] in self.metamodel_data:
                domain_results.update(self.metamodel_data[prompt['type']])

        # Build system prompt with multi-instance awareness
        system_prompt = self._build_multi_instance_prompt(prompt, is_multi_instance)

        chunks = self._chunk_document(document_content)
        logger.debug(f"Split document into {len(chunks)} chunks for {prompt['type']}")

        for i, chunk in enumerate(chunks, 1):
            logger.debug(f"Processing chunk {i}/{len(chunks)} for {prompt['type']}")
            print(f"  Processing part {i} of {len(chunks)}...", end="\r")
            
            try:
                chunk_results = self._process_chunk_with_instances(
                    chunk, 
                    prompt, 
                    system_prompt, 
                    previous_results,
                    is_multi_instance
                )
                
                if is_multi_instance:
                    # Merge new instances with existing ones
                    self._merge_instances(domain_results, chunk_results)
                else:
                    # Update single instance
                    self._merge_single_instance(domain_results, chunk_results)
                    
            except Exception as e:
                logger.error(f"Error processing chunk {i} for {prompt['type']}: {str(e)}")
                continue

        # Save results
        self._save_metamodel({prompt['type']: domain_results})
        
        print(f"  Completed {prompt['type']}                ")
        return domain_results

    def process_all_domains(self, document_path: str, prompts_path: str) -> Dict:
        """Process all domains sequentially with improved metamodel handling."""
        try:
            # Load document and prompts
            with open(document_path, 'r', encoding='utf-8') as f:
                document_content = f.read()
            
            with open(prompts_path, 'r', encoding='utf-8') as f:
                prompts_data = json.load(f)
            
            # Process each domain in sequence
            previous_results = self.metamodel_data.copy()  # Start with existing data
            
            for prompt in prompts_data['prompts']:
                logger.debug(f"Starting analysis for domain: {prompt['type']}")
                
                try:
                    domain_results = self.analyze_domain(
                        document_content=document_content,
                        prompt=prompt,
                        previous_results=previous_results
                    )
                    
                    # Update previous results for next iteration
                    previous_results[prompt['type']] = domain_results
                    
                    logger.debug(f"Completed analysis for domain: {prompt['type']}")
                    
                except Exception as e:
                    logger.error(f"Error processing domain {prompt['type']}: {str(e)}")
                    continue
            
            return self.metamodel_data
            
        except Exception as e:
            logger.error(f"Error in domain processing: {str(e)}")
            raise

def main():
    parser = argparse.ArgumentParser(description="Multi-domain organizational document analyzer")
    parser.add_argument('document', help='Path to the document to analyze')
    parser.add_argument('--prompts', default='All_domain_prompts_short.json',
                      help='Path to the prompts JSON file')
    parser.add_argument('--api-key', required=True,
                      help='Anthropic API key')

    args = parser.parse_args()

    try:
        analyzer = DomainAnalyzer(api_key=args.api_key)
        metamodel = analyzer.process_all_domains(args.document, args.prompts)
        
        print("\nAnalysis complete. Results saved to metamodel file.")
        
    except Exception as e:
        logger.error(f"Error during analysis: {str(e)}")
        raise

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

## 3.
Файл `All_domain_prompts_short.json`:
```
{
    "prompts": [
        {
            "type": "Organization",
            "task": "Analyze the document for an organization.",
            "definition": {
                "text": "An organization represents the structured entity within which activities and value delivery occur to achieve strategic objectives. It encompasses the interconnected units, roles, and responsibilities that enable the flow of value to customers, stakeholders, or outcomes. By analysing the organization's structure and its alignment with stakeholder needs, inefficiencies can be identified, improvements prioritised, and processes optimised to meet strategic priorities. Keywords to look for include: Organization, Structure, Roles, Responsibilities, and Alignment."
            },
            "extractionAndOutput": {
                "instructions": "For the organization, extract and collate details of the attributes listed below and output them to a JSON file.",
                "attributes": {
                    "Title": "The name or title of the organization.",
                    "Description": "A detailed explanation of what the organization entails.",
                    "Structure": "The organizational structure of the company.",
                    "Roles": "The roles within the organization.",
                    "Units": "Sub-divisions or units within the organization.",
                    "Mission Statement": "The mission of the organization.",
                    "Vision Statement": "The vision of the organization.",
                    "Values": "The core values guiding the organization.",
                    "Strategic Goals": "Long-term goals that align with the mission and vision.",

                    "Governance": "The governance framework and policies.",
                    "Legal Structure": "The legal structure of the organization.",

                }

            },

                    "Function": "A specific area of activity within an organization that performs a particular role or set of tasks.",

        {
            "type": "Value Streams",
            "task": "Analysis Process for VALUE STREAMS Documentation",
            "definition": {
                "text": "Value Streams transform inputs into valuable outcomes for stakeholders. A Value Stream represents an end-to-end series of activities that creates value, whether for external customers (like patients and communities) or internal stakeholders."
            },
            "extractionAndOutput": {
                "instructions": "For each instance of a value stream found, extract and collate details of the attributes listed below and output them to a JSON file.",
                "attributes": {
                    "Title": "The name or title of the value stream.",
                    "Description": "A detailed explanation of what the value stream entails.",
                    "Purpose": "The intended purpose or function of the value stream within the organization.",
                    "Owner": "The individual or team responsible for the value stream.",
                    "Organizational Unit": "The organizational unit(s) to which the value stream is linked.",
                    "Value Items": "Specific outputs or benefits generated at each stage.",
                    "Value Stream Stages": "Standards to evaluate performance at each stage.",
                    "Performance Indicators": "Metrics used to measure the effectiveness and efficiency of the value stream.",
                    "Dependencies": "Other capabilities, processes, or systems that the value stream depends on.",
                    "Related Value Streams": "Value streams that are related or linked to this value stream.",
                    "Maturity Level": "The current maturity level of the value stream.",
                    "Tools and Technologies": "Tools and technologies used to support or enable the value stream.",
                    "Compliance and Standards": "Regulatory requirements and standards the value stream must adhere to.",
                    "Cost": "The financial cost associated with implementing and maintaining the value stream.",
                    "Risks": "Potential risks associated with the value stream and its operations.",
                    "Improvement Opportunities": "Areas where the value stream can be enhanced or improved.",
                    "Strategic Alignment": "How the value stream aligns with the organization's strategic goals and objectives.",
                    "Parent": "A high-level capability that encompasses multiple sub-capabilities or child capabilities. It represents a broad area of business",
                    "Child": "A specific capability that falls under a broader parent capability. It represents a more granular aspect of business functionality."
                }

            "type": "Products",
            "task": "Analysis Process for PRODUCTS Documentation",
            "definition": {
                "text": "Products are distinct, tangible or intangible offerings created, developed, or delivered by an organization to address specific customer or stakeholder needs. Unlike services, which are activities or processes performed to deliver value, products are self-contained items or solutions that provide value independently or as part of a broader portfolio. Products are typically characterised by defined features, lifecycle stages, and measurable performance, whereas services are ongoing engagements or processes. To differentiate a product from a service: Product: Refers to a tangible or intangible deliverable that serves as a standalone solution to meet specific needs. Products are defined by their features, lifecycle stages, and ability to deliver measurable outcomes independently of ongoing interaction. Examples include software applications, physical goods, or digital tools. Service: Represents an activity or process performed to create or enhance value, often involving direct interaction and collaboration with customers or stakeholders. Services are dynamic, relying on real-time execution or customisation to address specific requirements. Examples include consulting, technical support, or training sessions. This distinction emphasises that products provide self-contained value, while services are centred around interactions and processes that generate or support value."

        "COMMISSIONER_OF_POLICE": {
            "Main": "In accordance with the Financial Management Act 1 994 , I am pleased to present the Victoria \nPolice Annual Report for the reporting period ending 30 June 2024. This report is a record \nof our  performance against a range of statutory requirements and the Government’s budget \nperformance measures. It includes an overview of our programs, initiatives, and achievements over \nthe past 1 2 months.\n2023–24 in review\nVictoria Police worked closely with communities and partner \nagencies to address key community safety concerns. This \ninvolved recognising and responding to social, economic and \ntechnological changes, as well as modernising key systems, \nprocesses and services.\nWe employed a range of proactive and specialist policing \nroles to prevent and respond to child and youth offending \nand victimisation.\nThrough the Embedded Youth Outreach Program (EYOP), \nwe provided a joint response to young people at high risk \nof antisocial or criminal behaviour and/or victimisation. The \nprogram aims to engage with at-risk young people and their \nfamilies before further offences or serious crimes take place.  \nThroughout 2023–24, we undertook work to expand EYOP , \nand on 1 July 2024, the program was successfully expanded \nto the Brimbank, Melton and Greater Shepparton areas. \nIn July 2023, we released the Victoria Police Youth Gang Strategy \n2023–2026 . The strategy directs our efforts to prevent youth \ngang membership and detect and disrupt violent and threatening \nbehaviour from youth gangs. Key areas of focus include \ngreater engagement and collaboration with the community \nand stakeholders through building police capacity and \ncapability to respond to the evolving youth gang environment. \nThe strategy complements and builds upon the work of \nOperation Alliance, our overarching operation targeting the \nharm caused by youth gangs and connected youth offenders. \nIn October 2023, Operation Park was established to oversee \noperational activities related to the Israel-Gaza conflict, with a \nfocus on policing protests and demonstrations related to the conflict. \nTaskforce Lunar was also established in response to an increasing \nnumber of arsons on tobacco stores and other premises linked to \norganised crime syndicates’ involvement in the illicit tobacco trade.\nWe are committed to a reduction in the representation of \nAboriginal people in the criminal justice system. Following my \nappearance before the Yoorrook Justice Commission in May \n2023, I felt it was important that I do everything within my power \nto make a real difference. In March 2024 I was proud to sign a Statement of Commitment to ensure we continue driving change \nby collaborating and building trust with Aboriginal community \nmembers. This Statement outlines the actions Victoria Police will \ntake from 2023 to 2025. We have partnered with Aboriginal \ncommunity members to ensure these actions are implemented, \nmonitored, and deliver tangible outcomes.\nRoad safety remains one of our key priorities. To keep the \ncommunity safe on our roads, we targeted intentional high-risk \ndriving, rural roads, speeding, impaired driving, seatbelts, restraints \nand distracted and unauthorised drivers. Throughout the year, \nwe conducted various operations to minimise road trauma risks, \ncontributing to the removal of dangerous drivers from Victorian \nroads and promoting general deterrence. Holidays continue to be \nhigh-risk periods on Victorian roads. In addition to local operations, \nwe delivered eight state-wide road policing operations to coincide \nwith major holiday periods and public holiday long weekends. \nIn our commitment to deliver a safer Victoria, we prioritise \ncommunity safety as the foundation of everything we do."

            "Main": "The Audit and Risk Committee is established under the Financial \nManagement Act  1994 . Its objective is to provide independent \nassurance and assistance to the Chief Commissioner of Police \nin relation to our risk, control and compliance framework, and \nexternal accountability responsibilities. \nThe committee has no executive powers, except those expressly \nprovided by the Standing Directions 20 1 8 (Standing Directions) \nof the Minister for Finance, and powers that may be delegated \nto it from time to time by the Chief Commissioner of Police. \nAll committee members are external to, and independent of, \nVictoria Police. Our internal \naudit program includes compliance and performance reviews, \nwith outcomes and mitigation actions reported to the Executive \nCommand Board and the Audit and Risk Committee."

        "Risk_management": {
            "Main": "Under Standing Direction 3.7 . 1 – Risk Management \nFramework and Processes , Victoria Police must apply the 2020 \nVictorian Government Risk Management Framework. We must \nconsider material risks in decision-making, and appropriately \nmanage risk taking into account our obligations, constraints, \nand objectives. Effective risk management enhances service \ndelivery and community safety outcomes.\nThe Victorian Government Risk Management Framework \ndescribes the minimum risk management requirement to \ndemonstrate that risks are managed effectively. It adopts \nthe ISO 3 1000:20 1 8 Risk management — Guidelines , \nan internationally accepted basis for best practice risk \nmanagement. \nRisk management obligations include the requirement that \nrisk considerations are documented and reflected in the \norganisation’s overall governance, strategic planning, \nperformance management, financial management and service \ndelivery processes and outcomes. \nThe Victoria Police Risk Management Framework aligns to \nthe Victorian Government Risk Management Framework \nand incorporates the Victoria Police Risk Appetite Statement \nand risk evaluation matrix. Strategic risks are managed \ncollaboratively and reported on regularly. Executive \nCommand Board recognises risk management as an integral \npart of organisational culture, policies, systems, and processes \nrequired to ensure community safety. Executive Command \nBoard is committed to advancing a positive risk culture to \nenhance understanding by all employees and contractors. \nThis will support employees to manage risks in their areas of \nresponsibilities through sound risk management practices.Strategic planning\nThe Keeping You Safe: Victoria Police Strategy 2023–2028 \n(Keeping You Safe) sets our organisational direction and focus. \nIt ensures that we continue to deliver policing, law enforcement \nand crime prevention activities in an evolving operational \nenvironment, keeping the Victorian community safe.\nWe will continue to realise the vision set out in Keeping You Safe \nthrough a suite of initiatives and projects under the pillars of:\n Policing — highly visible and responsive policing\n People — skilled people, ready and able to respond\n Partnerships — strong partnerships across community, \ngovernment and business.\nThese commitments are realised through activities undertaken \nby regions, departments, and commands, reflected in their \nannual plans and reported publicly in the Victoria Police \nCorporate Plan. Published annually in August, the Corporate \nPlan forms part of an integrated organisational planning \nframework and maintains focus across the organisation. It is \nrefreshed every year to reflect changes in government and \norganisational objectives, emerging priorities, and community \nsafety needs.\n8  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\n2 OUR PEOPLE\nVictoria Police is committed to building and maintaining a safe, respectful, \nand inclusive workplace and culture that embraces diversity and equality. \nThe health, safety and wellbeing of our people is integral to achieving \nour primary role of delivering a safer Victoria."

            "Main": "and inclusivity \nVictoria Police is committed to building and maintaining \na safe, respectful, and inclusive workplace by improving \nour culture and practices for the benefit of our people and \nthe community.Gender equality\nThe Equal, Safe & Strong: Victoria Police Gender Equality \nStrategy 2020–2030  sets out our plan to achieve sustainable \ngender equality in Victoria Police by 2030. The strategy \nprovides the foundation for regular action plans that will hold \nVictoria Police accountable for gender equality outcomes. \nEqual, Safe & Strong: Victoria Police Gender Equality Action \nPlan 2022–2024  identifies priorities and directs our efforts \nthrough 29 actions aligned to the following outcome areas:\n Our culture recognises and promotes the value of gender \nequality.\n Leaders take ownership and proactively sustain gender \nequality outcomes.\n The community has trust in our response to gendered \nviolence.\n Gender does not restrict access to flexible work, recruitment, \nor progression.\n Employees have confidence in our response to gendered \nworkplace harm.\n Gendered workplace harm is minimised.\nThere is a requirement under the Gender Equality Act  2020  \nthat a Gender Impact Assessment is applied to all new and \namended policies, programs and services. In accordance with \nthis and the Gender Equality Action Plan, there is a requirement \nfor Gender Impact Assessments to be applied to internal \npolicies, programs and services and this is now standard \nbusiness practice. Under the action plan, we also completed \nour two-yearly gender audit, as required by the Act, \ncreating a Monitoring, Evaluation and Learning Framework \nto measure outcomes towards the Gender Equality Action \nPlan and integrate learnings into the development of future \naction plans. \n|  9\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nOUR PEOPLE2Diversity and inclusion framework \nOur Diversity and Inclusion Framework 2023–2030  aims \nto bring diversity, inclusion, and a sense of purpose and \nbelonging into the leadership and operational frameworks of \nour organisation and supports the Gender Equality Strategy. \nThe framework identifies numerous actions that impact all \npriority diversity portfolios that are required to address diversity \nand inclusion culture and practices. The framework, which is \nsupported by four inclusion action plans, focuses on long-term \ntransformation, knowledge building, capability, performance, \nand accountability to promote a diverse and progressive \nworkforce.             "Main": "in our workforce\nDuring 2023–24, we held our second annual Aboriginal \nemployee forum.         "Youth Services": {
            "Main": "Matchworks\n Maurice Blackburn Lawyers\n Jobs Victoria\n Department of Education. \nThis program supported applicants from culturally-diverse \ncommunities (including Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander, \nMāori or Pasifika applicants and applicants who are of \nMiddle Eastern, East and South Asian, South American and \nAfrican heritage) to prepare and navigate the recruitment \nprocess to become police officers, PSOs or PCOs. \nThe program started in 20 1 8 and was expanded to a 1 5-week \naccredited course where students were taught entrance exam \npreparation skills, fitness, swimming and first aid. All participants \nwere matched with a Victoria Police mentor, attended a tour \nof the Victoria Police Academy and received presentations \nfrom guest speakers throughout the course. Upon completion, \nJesuit Social Services provided help during the recruitment \nphase and staff from our Centre for Law and Operational \nDevelopment coordinated ongoing help and mentoring \nduring training at the Victoria Police Academy. \nSince 20 1 8, 202 people from more than 53 cultures have \ncompleted the 1 5-week program. In 2023–24, 1 8 course \nparticipants graduated from the Victoria Police Academy, \nincluding 1 3 police officers, two PSOs and three PCOs.\nThe Victoria Police Diversity Recruitment Program formally \nconcluded on 30 June 2024. To keep the community safe on our roads, we target intentional high-\nrisk driving, rural roads, speeding, impaired driving, seatbelt and restraint non-compliance, distracted and unauthorised drivers.\nIn 2023–24, there were a total of 290 road fatalities in Victoria, which is an increase of 24 fatalities compared to the previous year. \nThe total number of road injuries decreased by nine per cent in 2023–24 compared to the previous year."

This \nmeasure excludes the effects of factors outside the control of \nVictoria Police such as revaluations (holding gains or losses) \narising from changes in market prices and other changes in \nthe volume of assets shown on the Comprehensive Operating \nStatement.\nVictoria Police Net result from transactions for 2023–24 is a \ndeficit of $33.0 million, compared to a surplus of $2.9 million \nin the previous financial year. \nTotal Income at $4.32 billion grew 3.6 per cent from the \nprevious year. The growth was mainly for the increasing \npolicing capacity to meet current and future demand, \nequipping frontline police officers with Conducted Energy \nDevices (CEDs) and Victoria Police system enhancements and \nresource initiatives. \nAdditional one-off funding came from the Australian \nGovernment to support policing services for the Association \nof Southeast Asian Nations (ASEAN) Special Summit held in \nMarch 2024, where the Hon. Anthony Albanese MP , Prime \nMinister of Australia hosted leaders of ASEAN and Timor-Leste. \nTotal Expenditure increased 4.5 per cent or $1 86.5 million \nthan the previous year. This was less than 1 per cent growth \nin expenditure compared to income, however, produced \na net result from transactions deficit of $33.0 million. There \nwere several key deficit drivers, including carryover of \naround 1 9,000 days of leave entitlements as a result of \nincreased operations and increased EBA entitlements overall. \nFurthermore, the annual WorkCover Premium rate increased \nfrom 5.56 per cent to 8.59 per cent in the year, predominantly \ndue to the rise in Mental Health claim injuries. The WorkCover \nscheme-wide rate also increased from 1.27 per cent to \n1.80 per cent which has impacted employers across Victoria. \nOverall, the WorkCover Premium additional cost for Victoria \nPolice was $87 .3 million in 2023–24. \nDuring the year both income and expenditure were impacted \nby the transfer of the Mobile Data Network, Metropolitan \nMobile Radio and  call-taking and dispatch services (Triple \nZero) to the Department of Justice and Community Safety."
        },

            "Main": "Conducted throughout August to October 2023, Operation \nEnforceable was a state-wide operation, targeting high-risk \nfamily violence offenders, that resulted in 22 1 2 enforceable \nactions. This included 598 arrests, 6 1 1 warrants executed, 4 16 \nwhereabouts cleared, and 587 intervention orders served. This \noperation was critical in demonstrating our commitment to our \ncommunity to prioritise victim-survivor safety, and to holding \nfamily violence perpetrators to account.Improved digital evidence gathering\nFrom June 2024, police officers can now access Axon \nCapture, an application which allows police officers to \nmore efficiently capture digital evidence such as photos, \nvideo and audio recordings through their mobile technology \ndevices. They can also upload it directly and in real-time to a \ncentralised storage location. The Axon Capture application \nalso allows members of the community to use a police-\nprovided link to directly upload their own digital evidence, \nsuch as photos and CCTV footage. This technology is saving \nsignificant time for police officers when transporting and \nmanaging digital evidence."

        "Operation_Park": {
            "Main": "Operation Park was established in October 2023 to oversee \noperational activities related to the Israel-Gaza conflict. \nA particular focus has been on policing protests and \ndemonstrations related to the conflict. These events have \nranged from weekly rallies in central Melbourne, which can \nattract up to several thousand attendees, to encampments \nat universities and smaller protests at businesses seen to \nbe supporting parties to the conflict. Since October 2023, \n435 protests and demonstrations have required police \nattendance and almost 1 2,000 police shifts have been \ndeployed to these events.   \nOther activities conducted under Operation Park included \ninvestigations of incidents motivated by prejudice, community \nreassurance patrols, community visits and meetings with key \ncommunity stakeholders.Cryptocurrency seizure \nPolice were granted new powers allowing them to gain control \nof cryptocurrency, on the spot or as soon as it is discovered, \nwhen changes to the Confiscation Act 1997  was enacted \non 1 August 2023. The Cryptocurrency Operations team \nhelped apply for a search warrant to further examine the \ncryptocurrency recovery phrases seized during a major drug \ntrafficking investigation. \nPolice seized multiple documents during the operation, that \nwere related to cryptocurrency “recovery phrases”. “Recovery \nphrases” are a series of words generated by an individual’s \ncryptocurrency wallet that allows the wallet to be copied and \ngiving access to the cryptocurreny associated with the wallet. \nVictoria Police detectives were able to access six wallets and \nseize $1 42,679. 10 worth of various cryptocurrencies. This was \nthe first successful cryptocurrency seizure since the changes \nto the legislation."
        },
        "Distracted_driver_and_seatbelt_camera_technology": {
            "Main": "Victoria Police supports the Department of Justice and \nCommunity Safety- led initiative under the Victorian \nGovernment’s Road Safety Action Plan 202 1-2023 . On 0 1 July \n2023, we started the formal use of distracted driver and seatbelt \ncamera technology. To use this technology, we must verify \nall images we use to issue infringements. From over 1 20,000 \ninfringements issued since 1 July 2023, less than 10 matters have \nbeen withdrawn due to incorrect verifications. The introduction \nof this technology has highlighted the prevalence of seatbelt \noffences (almost 45 per cent of offences detected, with the \nremaining 55 per cent being portable device offences). As a \nresult, we delivered publicity campaigns to raise awareness \nand focused our law enforcement activity. \n22  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nYEAR IN REVIEW4Streamlined Intervention Order application\nIn May 2024, we delivered a new centralised online application \nthat receives, stores and manages intervention orders (IVOs) \nand applications. This Streamlined Intervention Order (SIVO) \napplication provides police officers with state-wide 24-hour, \non-demand availability to all Victorian IVOs and applications. \nIt removes the dependency on the hard-copy IVOs faxed \nfrom court.  \nAvailable on both mobile and desktop devices, the SIVO \napplication gives police officers the ability to view details of all \nIVOs across the state, view service statuses relating to unserved \nIVOs, and generate notifications to involved parties of IVOs and \napplications. The earlier availability and visibility of IVOs and \napplications ensures all police have access to critical information \nto enable appropriate action within a timely manner.\nThe SIVO application is designed to enhance our response \nto family violence. It supports increased compliance with \nthe Family Violence Code of Practice and enhances safety, \nsecurity and community confidence."

        "Digital_Radio_Upgrade_Project": {
            "Main": "Police radios are an essential element of frontline policing. \nCompleted in June 2024, the Digital Radio Upgrade Project \nhas delivered approximately 1 4,000 new dual band digital \nradio devices, including thousands of new radio accessories \nand upgraded a wide range of vehicle and station-based \nradios.         "Disability_Liaison_Officers": {
            "Main": "In April 2024, the first group of Disability Liaison Officers \n(DLOs) started their new portfolio-based roles. DLOs are \npolice officers who help people with disability to access police \nservices. The 70 DLOs appointed to this new role are located \nacross the state, with one in every police service area, as well \nas in the Transit Safety Division and other specialist areas. \nThe DLOs were appointed through an expression of interest \nprocess, which attracted a high number of motivated and \ncommitted police officers. DLOs are mainly at Sergeant and \nSenior Sergeant rank, requiring demonstrated leadership skills, \naccountability, and commitment. \nThe DLO program was established to strengthen community \nconfidence in police. DLOs work with the broader disability \ncommunity in their local area, and through transfer of skills and \nknowledge, help their colleagues across the organisation to \nbetter engage with the disability community."

        "Neighbourhood_policing_forums": {
            "Main": "In 2023–24, 25 neighbourhood policing forums were held \nacross all four Victoria Police regions, covering metropolitan \nand rural police service areas. Held at the police service area \nlevel, these forums provide opportunity for engagement and \ncollaboration with stakeholders and community members. \nThey provide a platform for the community to ask questions \nand provide feedback directly on their community safety \nconcerns.\nThe dialogue at these forums helped us identify ways to \nenhance community engagement and safety. At each forum, \nlocal police officers worked with local schools, councils, \nbusinesses and support agencies to explore and implement \nimprovements which were identified. \n26  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nYEAR IN REVIEW4Youth Crime Prevention and Early Intervention Project\nThe Youth Crime Prevention and Early Intervention Project \n(YCPEIP) pilot continued in the North-West Metro Region. \nThe pilot involves working with local legal centres and support \nservices to enhance the use of police cautions and fast-tracked \ndiversions, reduce remand for children and young people, and \nensure they are referred to appropriate support programs. \nSince the YCPEIP pilot started in September 202 1, there have \nbeen encouraging results for youth incidents processed at \nthe pilot police stations. In Brimbank, the proportion of youth \nincidents resulting in remand in the 1 2-months prior to the pilot \nwas 46.8 per cent. In the 1 2-months to 3 1 August 2023, it \nhad dropped to 1 2 per cent. The effect was also observed in \nWyndham, with the proportion of remands decreasing from \n30.6 per cent in 202 1, to 6.5 per cent in 2023. During the \nsame period, the proportion of cautions increased from 6.4 per \ncent to 27 .7 per cent in Brimbank and 8.5 per cent to 33.2 per \ncent in Wyndham. The YCPEIP pilot’s results led to additional \ngovernment funding to expand the program across all four \nVictoria Police regions in 2024–25."
        },
        "Community_Integration_Support_Program": {
            "Main": "The Counter Terrorism Command continues to deliver \nthe Community Integration Support Program (CISP) in \npartnership with the Board of Imams Victoria. CISP is a \nvoluntary disengagement program that supports participants \nin custody or in the community who are assessed as being \nradicalised or at risk of radicalisation to religiously-motivated \nviolent extremism. \nCISP continues to deliver tailored intervention services \nto program participants including religious mentoring, \npsychological intervention, reintegration support, and family \nsupport. In March 2024, the Applied Security Science \nPartnership at Victoria University completed an external \nevaluation of CISP titled Facilitating disengagement from \nviolent extremism: Evidence from the CISP . The research \nprovided several key findings that support the high success rate \nof the CISP in engaging convicted terrorists in the program. \nThe research findings will inform projects related to CISP in \n2024–25 to ensure continuous improvement of the program."
        },
        "Victoria_Police_SHIELD": {
            "Main": "Victoria Police SHIELD is an online platform, allowing \ninformation to be shared between the public and private \nsecurity sectors, with the aim of preventing terrorism. SHIELD \nworks to strengthen partnerships in the public and private \nsecurity sector. This is achieved through information sharing on \nthe current threat environment, and best practices in protecting \ncrowded places from terrorism.  \nIn 2023–24, SHIELD expanded these partnerships, increasing \nthe number of members and targeting sectors with limited \nrepresentation. SHIELD also proactively engaged with \npartners to share information and ideas that support the \nprotection of crowded places from terrorism through the delivery of “crowded places” forums and targeted awareness \nsessions. To raise awareness on current themes across the \nterrorism landscape, SHIELD published fortnightly environment \nannouncements, monthly global terrorism statistics, incident and \ndisruptive demonstration activity notifications and reports."

        "Operation_Tiedown": {
            "Main": "Operation Tiedown identified an organised crime network \ninvolved in the importation, manufacture, and trafficking of \ndrugs into and across Australia. The operation is an example \nof a joint agency operation with staff from Victoria Police, \nAustralian Federal Police, Australian Border Force and the \nAustralian Criminal Intelligence Commission (ACIC).\nOn 1 5 August 2023, the Australian Border Force intercepted \na consignment, locating 78 kilograms of resin blocks \nfilled with methamphetamines concealed within the body \nof surfboards. Further investigation identified a second \nsuspicious consignment of plastic moulds, which was seized \nunder a search warrant.  Australian Federal Police forensic \nofficers found the moulds were filled with 1 54 1 kilograms of \nmethamphetamines. This seizure is one of the largest seizures \nof methamphetamine in Victoria. Our detectives played a key \nrole in the operation, which resulted in these significant seizures \nand the arrests of two alleged members of an international \norganised crime syndicate. The men, from Taiwan and China, \nwere both charged with importing a border-controlled drug."
        },
        "Virtual_kidnappings": {
            "Main": ""
        },
        "Virtual_kidnappings_are_a_scam_commonly_affecting": {
            "Main": "international students. Scammers impersonate government \nauthorities or military officials to convince victims they are in \nlegal trouble or are implicated in a crime. To resolve the issue, \nthey are pressured to pay large sums of money to overseas \nbank accounts. Through coercion, victims are led to stage their \nown kidnapping to convince family and friends to hand over \nthe large sums of money. \nWe identified a spike in virtual kidnappings, and in late \n2023 to early 2024, we engaged with Victorian universities \nand state-based agencies who have contact with the \ninternational student cohort. We provided education about \nvirtual kidnappings and collaborated with both mainstream \nand Chinese-speaking media outlets, to inform and address \nany community safety concerns. Following our engagement, \nwe have seen a significant reduction in reported virtual \nkidnapping incidents."
        },

        "Local_Jobs_First": {
            "Main": "The Local Jobs First Act 2003  brings together the Victorian \nIndustry Participation Policy (VIPP) and Major Project \nSkills Guarantee (MPSG) policy, which were previously \nadministered separately.\nAs a public sector body, Victoria Police is required to apply the \nLocal Jobs First Policy in all our procurement activities valued at \n$3 million or more in metropolitan Melbourne or state-wide; \nor valued at $1 million or more for procurement activities in \nregional Victoria.\nThe MPSG requirements apply to all Victorian Government \nconstruction projects valued at or above $20 million. \nThe MPSG and VIPP guidelines will continue to apply to \napplicable projects where contracts have been entered into, \nprior to 1 5 August 20 1 8.\nProjects commenced – Local Jobs First Standard\nIn 2023–24, six contracts with a combined commercial value \nof $102.5 million and applicable Local Jobs First commitments \ncommenced. Five contracts were located within metropolitan \nMelbourne and one in regional Victoria. Commitments \nincluded local content, local jobs created and retained, and \nthe use of small-to-medium enterprises in the supply chain.\nThe outcomes expected from the implementation of the Local \nJobs First Policy are:\n a commitment to an average of 46.8 per cent of local \ncontent; and\n 1 46 small-to-medium enterprises included in the \nsupply chain.\nProjects completed – Local Jobs First Standard\nWe did not complete any Local Jobs First Standard Projects \nduring 2023–24. Local Jobs First strategic projects\nA Local Jobs First strategic project is a project with a budget \nof $50 million or more, or any other project declared to be \na strategic project by the Minister under section 7A (2) of \nthe Act. \nThere were no strategic projects where Local Jobs First \ncommitments applied.\nReporting requirements – grants\nWe did not provide any grants during 2023–24."

        "Social_procurement_framework": {
            "Main": "The Victorian Social Procurement Framework applies \nto procurement of all goods, services and construction \nundertaken by, or on behalf of, departments and agencies \nsubject to the Standing Directions under the Financial \nManagement Act 1994 . It enables organisations to use their \nbuying power to generate social value above and beyond the \nvalue of the goods, services, or construction being procured. \nThe framework establishes seven social procurement \nobjectives: \n opportunities for Victorian Aboriginal people \n opportunities for Victorians with disability \n women’s equality and safety \n opportunities for Victorian priority jobseekers \n supporting safe and fair workplaces \n sustainable Victorian social enterprise and Aboriginal \nbusiness sectors \n sustainable Victorian regions. \nIt also establishes three sustainable procurement objectives: \n environmentally-sustainable outputs\n environmentally-sustainable business practices \n implementation of the climate change policy objectives. \n|  29\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nOTHER DISCLOSURES5Since establishing and implementing our first social procurement \nstrategy in 20 1 9, we have prioritised the following objectives: \n sustainable Victorian social enterprise and Aboriginal \nbusiness sectors, through increased purchasing from \nVictorian social enterprises and Aboriginal businesses \n women’s equality and safety, where all suppliers engaged \nare required to demonstrate equitable employment practices \nand family violence leave or the development of same \n supporting safe and fair workplaces, where all suppliers \nengaged are required to demonstrate that they comply with \nindustrial relations laws and promote secure employment. In 2023–24, we spent a total of $1.345 million with 34 social \nbenefit suppliers. A total of $205,376 was spent with 1 4 \nVictorian Aboriginal businesses. Data on supplier performance \nagainst women’s equality and safety and supporting safe and \nfair workplaces is not available for this reporting period. \nWhile sustainable procurement objectives have not been the \nprimary focus of our social procurement strategy, we have \nincorporated environmental considerations into procurement \ndecision-making where possible.         "Compliance_with_the_Freedom_of": {
            "Main": "Information Act 1982 \nThe Freedom of Information Act 1982  gives people the \nright to request access to information held by the Victorian \nGovernment and its agencies, including personal documents \nand documents relating to government activities. The Act \nalso allows people to request that incorrect or misleading \ninformation regarding their personal affairs held by an \nagency is amended and provides an appeal process to \nreview access decisions.\nIn 2023–24, we received 3903 requests for access to \ndocuments under the Act, excluding requests from applicants \nto amend personal information. Of those applications, 3344 \nrelated to personal requests and 559 were non-personal \nrequests. Victoria Police received 1 5 applications from \nMembers of Parliament, 35 media requests and the remaining \n3853 were from the public and public interest groups.\nSince 1 December 20 1 2, reviews of decisions made \nunder the Act are the responsibility of the Office of the \nVictorian Information Commissioner. Details of the number \nof applications for review and the decisions are published \nin the Office of the Victorian Information Commissioner’s \nannual report.\nIn 2023–24, there were 45 appeals lodged with the Victorian \nCivil and Administrative Tribunal (VCAT) for final determination, \n20 cases were withdrawn and 25 were not yet finalised by \n30 June 2024."
        },
        "Requests_for_access_to_documents": {
            "Main": ""
        },
        "Type_of_Request_Number": {
            "Main": "Personal 3344\nNon-personal 559\nTotal requests 3903\n2023–24 Appeals to VCAT 45\n2023–24 Appeals actually determined and outcome 0Compliance\nIn 2023–24, we processed 1 9 per cent of requests within the \nstatutory time period, 8 per cent within one to 45 days after \nthe statutory time period, and 73 per cent were completed in \n46 or more days after the statutory time period. The average \nprocessing time taken to finalise requests was 282 days. \nOur compliance performance was impacted by an existing \nbacklog of requests."

        "Competitive_neutrality_policy": {
            "Main": "The Victorian Government’s Competitive Neutrality Policy is to \nensure that significant government businesses compete fairly \nin the market. The policy sets out the Victorian Government’s \napproach to competitive neutrality. Victoria Police ensures it \nfulfils its requirements on competitive neutrality reporting as \nrequired under the Competition Principles Agreement and \nCompeitition and Infrastructure Reform Agreement. Compliance with the Public \nInterest Disclosure Act 2012 \nThe purpose of the Public Interest Disclosure Act 20 1 2  is to \nencourage and facilitate the reporting of improper conduct \nand corruption committed by public officers and public bodies \nand to ensure those reporting such behaviours are protected \nagainst detrimental action. Detrimental action includes \ndiscrimination, disadvantage, or adverse treatment directed \ntowards the person who made the disclosure of improper \nconduct and corruption. Victoria Police does not tolerate \nimproper conduct by employees, including detrimental action \nagainst those coming forward to report such conduct. \nVictoria Police is committed to ensuring transparency and \naccountability in administrative and management practices \nand supporting the making of disclosures that reveal improper \nconduct including corrupt conduct, criminal offences, or serious \nprofessional misconduct. We will continue to take all reasonable \nsteps to protect persons who make such disclosures from \ndetrimental action and afford natural justice to the person who is \nthe subject of the disclosure to the extent it is legally possible."
        },
        "Reporting_procedures": {
            "Main": "Any person may make a disclosure about the conduct of \nVictoria Police employees.\nA complaint may be made orally or in writing (by post or \nelectronically) to a member of Victoria Police.  \nA Victoria Police employee may make a complaint about \nanother employee, or report circumstances they believe may \ninvolve an ethics or integrity issue by making the complaint \ndirectly to a police member of a more senior rank than the \nemployee making the complaint, to Professional Standards \nCommand or to the IBAC.\nA Victoria Police employee making a disclosure is encouraged \nto advise their direct or indirect manager or supervisor. \nTo make a public interest complaint visit: police.vic.gov.au/\ncompliments-and-complaints\nAlternatively, disclosures may be number and types of public interest complaints dismissed by the \ninvestigating entity;0 (d)\nSection 69(c) the number of applications for an injunction made by the investigating entity \nunder section 50 during the financial year0 (d)\nNotes:  \nOne disclosure may contain multiple allegations.\n(a) Section 69(1)(b)(i) the number and types of disclosures notified to the IBAC under section 2 1(2) or 22 relates to files that Victoria Police refer to the IBAC as assessable \ndisclosures. The two sections differentiate between who made the complaint:\n• Section 2 1 disclosures relate to complaints from the public/VPS (employee/police custody officer). These complaints may be about police officers or other \nemployees within Victoria Police.\n• Section 22 police complaints relate to complaints about police against police/police against PSO.\n(b) Section 69(1)(b)(ii) the number and types of public interest complaints referred to the investigating entity by IBAC. This relates to files that the IBAC refer back to Victoria \nPolice for investigation. These are referrals made to Victoria Police under Section 73 of the Independent Broad-based Anti-corruption Act 20 1 1 (Section: Referral of \ncomplaint or notification for investigation by another person or body). This will include matters that Victoria Police has referred to the IBAC as “assessable disclosures”. \nThese referrals have been classified as public interest complaints by IBAC. This will also include complaints made directly to the IBAC about the IBAC, these matters will \nbe classified as public interest complaints and referred to Victoria Police for investigation (as opposed to the IBAC investigating or dismissing it).\n(c) Section 69(1)(b)(iii) the number and types of public interest complaints investigated by the investigating entity. This relates to the number of complaints that Victoria \nPolice investigates once they have been referred to Victoria Police from IBAC. Victoria Police investigates all referrals as Victoria Police cannot dismiss or refer to another \nagency for investigation. Victoria Police are not an investigating entity that can dismiss a public interest disclosure.\n(d) Victoria Police cannot dismiss a public interest disclosure or process an injunction."

        "Compliance_with_the_Carers": {
            "Main": "Recognition Act 2012\nWe recognise the contribution made to the community by carers. \nWe have taken measures to promote and support those with \ncaring responsibilities under the Carers Recognition Act 20 1 2 .\nWe promote the availability of flexible work arrangements \nand in line with the Act, we support flexibility while aiming to \nminimise likelihood of efficiency losses or significant negative \nimpact on customer service, productivity, or business continuity.We promote carer’s leave entitlements along with other leave \nentitlements, in line with enterprise agreements, policy, and the \nFair Work Act 2009 . We ensure our compliance by providing \nclear and consistent advice to employees and managers.\nWe provide support through the Employee Assistance Program, \nas well as the Carer and Parent Hub on the Victoria Police \nintranet. The hub is tailored to the needs and responsibilities of \ncarers and provides practical guidance and resources to help \nbalance carer responsibilities with work commitments.\n|  35\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nOTHER DISCLOSURES5Compliance with the Disability \nAct 2006\nWe acknowledge that people with disability face significant \nbarriers in their access to justice and often experience \ndiscrimination and harm. As an entry point into the justice \nsystem, we aim to be accessible to everyone.\nVictoria Police  acknowledges the importance of being \naccessible and ensures the Victoria Police Accessibility Action \nPlan 202 1–2023 meet all legislative requirements under \nsection 38 of the Disability Act 2006 (the Act) . Between \nNovember 2023 and February 2024, extensive community \nconsultation was undertaken to inform the development of the \nsubsequent Accessibility Action Plan 2024–2026.  \nThe new Accessibility Action Plan  is currently being finalised \nand the timing of its release is subject to the timing of a whole of \ngovernment response to the Royal Commission into Violence, \nAbuse, Neglect, and Exploitation of People with Disability.\nProgress of Accessibility Action Plan  actions during  \n2023–24 included:\n training media and communications staff in digital \naccessibility\n improving access to police stations through a police station \nlocator tool on the Victoria Police website.\n establishing the Disiability Liaison Officer (DLO) portfolio-\nbased role, with 70 DLOs now in place across the state \nand supported by a Community of Practice\n hosting the annual Office of the Public Advocate Awards \nrecognising employees, work units and stations that made \noutstanding contributions to improving outcomes for \npeople with disability\n supporting employees to volunteer their time in support \nof the Special Olympics through Law Enforcement Torch \nRun activities throughout the year ensuring an employee with experience of disability is \nincluded on recruitment panels when a person with disability \nhas requested support, facilitated through our Victoria Police \nEnablers employee network.\nOur Access and Inclusion Action Plan 2023–2024  operates \nin conjunction with the Accessibility Action Plan  and contains \ncommitments in compliance with section 38(1)(b) of the Act \nto reduce barriers to persons with disability obtaining and \nmaintaining employment.\nAchievements in this area related to workforce inclusion during \n2023–24 included:\n updating the Reasonable Adjustment Passport, including \ndevelopment of a digital passport to support information \ntechnology (IT) related adjustments\n renewing our Disability Confident Recruiter accreditation in \nconjunction with the Australian Disability Network\n hosting six “Stepping Into” internships, of which four have \nsuccessfully gained subsequent employment with us\n ensuring our Employee Assistance Program providers are \ndisability confident."
        },

The \nservice, based at the Melbourne Custody Centre, has 45 \npermanent staff consisting of  doctors, nurses, pharmacists, and \nadministration staff. In regional Victoria, 1 7 contracted doctors \nattend to patients in their local police station. \nServices are coordinated through the Custodial Health Advice \nLine, a 24 hours a day 7 days a week telephone service staffed \nby nurses, which receives calls and emails from all police stations \nregarding people in custody and their health issues. The health \ncare required varies, from telephone advice, a nurse assessment \nor a doctor assessment, depending on the nature of the health \nproblem. We also conduct telehealth consultations. The in-house \npharmacy dispenses or coordinates with community pharmacies \nto provide any required medication. \nIn 2023–24 we recorded over 2 1,000 episodes of care for \npeople in custody. Of those, 8700 underwent an initial face-\nto-face nurse assessment.         "Greenhouse_gas_emissions": {
            "Main": "In line with the state-wide transition to net zero emissions by \n2045, we are participating in actions to reduce emissions \nfrom our operations. These actions form part of the whole of \ngovernment pledge under the Climate Change Act 20 1 7 .  \nConsistent with national and international reporting standards, \ngreenhouse gas emissions are broken down into “scopes”:\n Scope 1 greenhouse gas emissions are from sources that \nwe own or control, such as burning fossil fuels through use \nof our vehicles or machinery.\n Scope 2 greenhouse gas emissions are indirect emissions \nfrom our use of electricity from the grid, where non-\nrenewable power generation is used.\n Scope 3 greenhouse gas emissions are indirect emissions \nfrom corporate air travel and waste disposal.\nOur Scope 1 greenhouse gas emissions cannot be directly \ncompared to previous reporting periods, as we now collect \nand report more data than we did in previous years. For \nexample, the 2023–24 report includes the most complete \ndata for aircraft greenhouse gas emissions, used in the \nreporting to date. \nScope 2 greenhouse gas emissions decreased by 16.6 per \ncent in 2023–24 compared to 2022–23. Light-emitting \ndiode (LED) installation at 67 sites contributed to the reduction \nin greenhouse gas emissions due to reduced electricity \nconsumption. The significant reduction in the August 2023 \nAustralian National Greenhouse Accounts Factors for \nelectricity generation and distribution have contributed to \nthe reduction in scope 2 greenhouse gas emissions.\nScope 3 greenhouse gas emissions increased by 22.7 \nper cent in 2023–24 compared to 2022–23. This reflects \nan increase in waste data collection rather than an actual \nincrease in emissions.\nIndicator 202 1–22 2022–23 2023–24\nG1. Total Scope 3 greenhouse gas emissions from commercial air travel and waste \ndisposal (Tonnes CO2-e)2498 3137 3849\nNotes:  \n(a) Direct comparisons to previous reporting periods cannot be made for Scope 1 emissions due to an increase in data coverage in 2023–24 in areas including buildings \nand non-road vehicle data. \n|  43\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nOTHER DISCLOSURES5Electricity production and consumption\nMost of Victoria Police’s energy consumption falls within three \nkey areas:\n lighting \n heating, ventilation, and air-conditioning\n information and communication technology. \nElectricity energy consumption reduced by 2.7 per cent from \n2022–23 to 2023–24. A lighting upgrade project completed in \nJune 2023 at 67 facilities has subsequently resulted in a decrease \nin electricity consumption by on average 9.7 per cent at these sites, equating to an approximate 1 879 tonne reduction in greenhouse \ngas emissions, with some sites reducing gas consumption by \napproximately 20 per cent. The project has improved the energy \nefficiency of existing buildings, reducing operating costs and \ngreenhouse gas emissions from electricity consumption.\nThe 2023–24 data accounts for 98.7 per cent of sites and \n98. 1 per cent of Victoria Police FTE.\nWith the reduction in electricity consumption and improved \nelectricity emission factors, the greenhouse gas emissions for \nelectricity reduced by 16.6 per cent in 2023–24.\nIndicator 202 1–22 2022–23 2023–24\nEL1.         "Stationary_fuel_use": {
            "Main": "Sources of emissions from stationary fuel include natural gas and \nliquid petroleum gas (LPG) used in some buildings, diesel used \nfor some machinery such as back-up generators for facilities and \nportable generators for operations. We collected data primarily \nthrough billing information from fuel suppliers. For natural gas, \ndata accounts for 100 per cent of sites that are utilising gas, \nwhich represents accommodation for 87 per cent of FTE.\nStationary fuel use decreased by 1 2.0 per cent from 2022–23 \nto 2023–24. The decrease in natural gas consumption was driven by two factors. Firstly, reduced gas consumption at the \nVictoria Police Centre from the failure of the co-generation \nunit for the entire reporting period for 2023–24. Secondly, \nthe 2023 winter was the warmest winter on Bureau of \nMeteorology records, which appears to have contributed \nto reducing gas consumption at over 80 Victoria Police sites. \nThe Victoria Police Centre consumption accounted for  \n1 9 per cent of total Victoria Police gas consumption, therefore \nthe reduction in gas consumption at this site heavily influenced \nthe overall results.\nIndicator 202 1–22 2022–23 2023–24\nF1. We are continuing \nto implement a vehicle telematics solution into our fleet. This \ntechnology will help us improve safety for our staff and better \nmanage vehicle use. As of 30 June 2024, 2305 telematic \ndevices have been installed throughout the current fleet. This will \nallow for more efficient utilisation of the fleet, and in turn should \nassist in reducing greenhouse gas emissions and inform planning \nand exploration into lower carbon emissions vehicles. Our data reporting capability includes metrics on non-road \nvehicles. Since 2022–23, comparisons for \ntransportation emissions are not appropriate due to changes \nin data coverage.         "Sustainable_buildings_and_infrastructure": {
            "Main": "We play a critical role in responding to extreme weather \nevents, such as floods and fires, and have been directly \nimpacted with loss and damage to police buildings and \nvehicles. Extreme weather events in recent years, such as \nthe 20 1 9–20 bushfires, and the 2020 and 2022 floods \ndemonstrate the importance of addressing climate change risk, \nparticularly when it comes to the design and management of \nbuildings and infrastructure assets.  \nWe are committed to efficiency and reducing adverse impacts \non the environment and continue to proactively identify, \ndevelop, and implement initiatives, to reduce greenhouse gas \nemissions and adapt to the changing climate.\nAll new facilities are designed using sustainable design \nprinciples, and resource efficient features. As stipulated in \nVictoria’s Climate Change Strategy , from 202 1, all new \nVictoria Police buildings and tenancy fit-outs will have \nembedded environmentally sustainable design with a minimum \nfive-star Green Star energy performance rating. This will \nincrease to six-star in 2025.\nNew leased premises are acquired through the Department of \nGovernment Services’ Accommodation, Carpool and Library \nServices (ACLS) which ensures fit-outs for new leased premises \nachieve and maintain a National Australian Built Environment Rating System (NABERS) 5-star rating (without green power), or \nhigher. Industry standards must also be adopted to support best \npractice sustainability and user health outcomes throughout the \ndifferent phases of design, fit-out and ongoing operation. Where \nan office fit-out is unable to achieve this rating, ACLS must \ndemonstrate that a “best endeavours” approach has been taken \nby implementing as many as possible of the design components \nof the NABERS five-star rating.\nVictoria Police established a monitoring system to ensure a \ncontinued reduction in water and energy consumption, a \nreduction in waste levels, and improvement to the quality of the \nindoor environment of the Victoria Police Centre. The centre \noperates as an environmentally sustainable building that was \ndesigned to high environmentally sustainable design standards \nusing certified products."
        },

Under the \nprogram, takeaway cups are upcycled to produce other items, \nsuch as re-usable cups, car park stops and roadside kerbing, \noutdoor furniture, air-conditioning mounting blocks, road \nsurfacing and lightweight concrete. \nIndicator 202 1–22 % 2022–23 % 2023–24 %\nWR1. The \ncode aims to ensure all suppliers adhere to minimum standards \nof ethical, sustainable, and socially responsible practices \nwhen providing goods or services. Under these requirements, \nsuppliers must acknowledge the code by signing and returning \na commitment letter as part of the procurement process and if \nsuccessful, as part of their supply of the goods and/or services. \nConsistent with the sustainable procurement objectives of the \nVictorian Social Procurement Framework, Victoria Police has \nincorporated environmental considerations into procurement \ndecision-making where possible.\nVictoria Police is committed to and supports environmental \nsustainability. Factors such as material minimisation, use of \nrecycled materials and material re-use are incorporated along \nwith mandatory requirements of product handling, distribution \nand system processing when assessing tender responses. While \nno weighting is apportioned to these considerations unless \nminimum thresholds apply, tenderers are asked to describe how \nthey incorporate environmental sustainability into their business \npractices. Standard Request for Tender clauses explore \noptions to recycle and/or reduce the environmental impact \nof packaging and minimise the amount of waste that goes to \nlandfill.\nIn 2023–24, 29 suppliers committed to the mandatory Supplier \nCode of Conduct which stipulates the minimum standards of \nethical, sustainable, and socially responsible practices, 23 \ncontracts were obtained from mandated whole of Victorian \nGovernment purchase contracts, where corporate, social, \nand environmental requirements already apply and 59 \nVictoria Police construction projects contractually require our \ncontractors to comply with Volume One, Section J (Energy \nEfficiency) of the National Construction Code."

        "Framework_maturity_assessment": {
            "Main": "The following sections summarise our assessment of maturity \nagainst the requirements of the Asset Management Accountability \nFramework (AMAF). The AMAF is a non-prescriptive, \ndevolved accountability model of asset management that \nrequires compliance with 4 1 mandatory requirements. \nThese requirements can be found on the DTF webpage:  \ndtf.vic.gov.au/infrastructure-investment/asset-management-\naccountability-framework\nOur target maturity rating is “competence”, which is a state \nwhere asset management systems and processes are fully \nin place across the asset lifecycle, from asset acquisition, \nperformance management, maintenance, critical failure, \nthrough to disposal. To reach “competence”, these systems and \nprocesses need to be consistently and systematically applied \nto meet the AMAF requirements. This includes a continuous improvement process to expand system performance above \nAMAF minimum requirements. Assurance activities must \nconsider the size, criticality and service delivery objectives of \neach type of asset.\nThe 2023–24 Victoria Police AMAF compliance assessment \nconcludes that compliance is attained with 23 of the 4 1 \nrequirements (see chart below). There is partial compliance with \n1 8 requirements and there is no material compliance deficiency. \nThe areas of focus for improvement relate to the capture of \nasset information and the application of asset information to \ninvestment planning, including to better consider the risks from \nclimate change. \n2023–24 Asset Management Accountability"
