        "Firearms_Register": {
            "Main": "continue working towards the implementation of the \nrecommendations relevant to Victoria Police from the Victorian \nGovernment’s 2020 review of the private security industry\n continue work to integrate some services for firearms and \nprivate security licensing with Service Victoria.\n50  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nOTHER DISCLOSURES5These key objectives support our ongoing endeavours to \nmodernise regulatory practices. As members of the Firearms \nand Weapons Policy Working Group, we work closely with  \ncommonwealth, state and territory agencies towards full \nintegration of the National Firearms Register into our systems \nand processes. \nWe are also continuing collaborative work with Service \nVictoria to introduce an end-to-end digital service for selected \nlicensing application processes.\nIn March 2024, amendments to the Private Security Act 2004  \npassed in the Victorian Parliament receiving Royal Assent in \nApril 2024. The amendments follow recommendations from \nthe Victorian Government’s 2020 review of the private security \nindustry. Changes include transitioning to a streamlined \nlicensing scheme for all private security activities. Additionally, \nthe amendments provide a broader pathway for making \ncomplaints and introduce new offences relating to sub-\ncontracting of security activities. All amendments to the Act \nwill be enacted by 1 9 June 2025.\nWe are currently developing plans to implement these \nchanges to the private security licensing and regulation \nframework. As part of the implementation of new legislative \nprovisions, we are working with the Victorian Government to \ndevelop guidance for private security operators to implement \nstandards for risk assessments and a code of conduct for all \nlicence holders. We have also worked with the Department of \nJustice and Community Safety to introduce a new factsheet for \nworkers within the private security industry. The factsheet will be \nincluded in all new private security licence applications and \nrenewals. This will assist workers to understand their rights at \nwork and the supports available. \nRegulating the firearms, weapons and private \nsecurity industries\nVictoria Police values its role as the regulator of the firearms, \nweapons and private security industries.         "INITIATIVES_AND_OUTCOMES": {
            "Main": "The Chief Commissioner made a commitment to the Yoorrook Justice \nCommission that real change would take place in Victoria Police in its \ndealings with the Victorian Aboriginal community. This chapter reports \non these actions, initiatives and outcomes. \nOn 8 May 2023, the Chief Commissioner appeared before \nthe Yoorrook Justice Commission and apologised to the \nVictorian Aboriginal Community for actions by police over the \nhistory of the organisation which have caused significant harm \nacross generations of Aboriginal families.\nReleased publicly in May 2024, the Chief Commissioner’s \nStatement of Commitment includes 79 accompanying \nactions to ensure that real change flows from his commitments \nmade at the Yoorrook Justice Commission. The Statement of \nCommitment sets out areas where we will take action between \n2023 and 2025. It can be found at: police.vic.gov.au/\nstatement-commitment\nThe Chief Commissioner’s Statement of Commitment broadly \ncovers three themes: \n monitoring and accountability\n cultural competence and human rights capability\n human rights and cultural rights compliance. \nThe introduction of this chapter aims to highlight Victoria \nPolice Aboriginal-focused initiatives and outcomes, thereby \naddressing action 1 5 of the Statement of Commitment. As of \n30 June 2024, 1 5 of the 79 Statement of Commitment actions \nhave been formally acquitted by the Victoria Police First \nPeoples Committee."
        },
        "Victoria_Police_First_Peoples_Committee": {

The \nFirst Peoples Committee comprises of senior employees, who \nhave responsibility for Statement of Commitment actions. The \ncommittee is co-chaired by a Senior Aboriginal Community \nRepresentative and the Deputy Commissioner, Capability. \nThe committee’s lifespan is aligned with the Chief \nCommissioner’s commitment to have the actions in the \nStatement of Commitment acquitted by 2025.Installation of the Yoorrook Justice"

It stands there as a visual \nreminder to all current and future Victoria Police officers of \ntheir duty to protect “First Peoples communities and families: to \nprotect First Peoples culture, cultural rights and human rights.”"

        "Apology_to_the_Stolen_Generations": {
            "Main": "On 24 May 2024, the Chief Commissioner delivered an \napology to Stolen Generations survivors and their families at \nthe Aborigines Advancement League.\nThe Chief Commissioner apologised for Victoria Police’s role \nin the removal of Aboriginal children from their families and \ncommunities. Through this apology to the Stolen Generations, \nVictoria Police accepted responsibility for the widespread \nharm caused to Aboriginal people by the role police played \nin forcibly removing children from their families, and the deep \nimpact this had on severing their connection to country, culture, \nkin and identity. \nVictoria Police also recognised the impact to the children of \nthose who were removed and generations that come after them.\nThe Chief Commissioner made this apology to honour Victoria \nPolice’s deep commitment to a genuine truth-telling process that:\n acknowledges the causes of injustices\n creates an opportunity to heal \n brings meaningful and positive change.\n|  57\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nABORIGINAL-FOCUSED INITIATIVES AND OUTCOMES6Aboriginal Community Liaison Officers\nThe Aboriginal Community Liaison Officer (ACLO) role was \nimplemented in 2005 to address recommendation 23 1 of \nthe Royal Commission into Aboriginal Deaths in Custody, to \nstrengthen relationships and facilitate communication between \nVictoria Police and Aboriginal people across the State. \nThe ACLO program is part of a strategy to increase \nemployment opportunities for Aboriginal people within Victoria \nPolice, aligning with the intent of the Statement of Commitment. \nACLOs are Victorian Public Service employees attached to \nPolice Service Areas who perform liaison roles to foster trust, \nunderstanding and respect between Victoria Police and the \nAboriginal Community. \nIn 2023–24, in order to support areas that have demonstrated \na need for an ACLO, two additional roles were created in \nHorsham and the City of Yarra. These new roles assist in the \ndelivery of existing programs and strategies including: \n decriminalisation of public drunkenness\n Aboriginal Youth Cautioning Program\n Aboriginal Community Justice Panels\n Police and Aboriginal Community Protocols Against"
        },
        "Family Violence": {
            "Main": "Aboriginal Cultural Awareness Training.\nAs of July 2024, there were 1 7 ACLO positions across both \nregional and metropolitan areas in Victoria.\nAn independent review of the ACLO role has been undertaken \nand the roles have been reclassified to reflect the importance \nand the expanded responsibilities of the role."
        },
        "Senior_Aboriginal_Programs_Coordinator": {
            "Main": "The creation of the Senior Aboriginal Programs Coordinator \nposition in late 2023 has acquitted Action 33 of the Statement \nof Commitment. \nThe Senior Aboriginal Programs Coordinator position is a \ndesignated position which provides centralised strategic and \ncultural support for the ACLOs. In accordance with section \n1 2 of the Equal Opportunities Act 20 10 , only Aboriginal \nand Torres Strait Islander people are eligible to apply \nfor designated positions. This position was developed in \naccordance with not only the Statement of Commitment but \nalso the Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Inclusion Action \nPlan 2023–2025 . The role is key in enabling the development \nof consistent governance and service delivery.\nThis role provides the following benefits:\n information sharing across the ACLO and Police Aboriginal \nLiaison Officers (PALOs) networks\n enhanced organisational capacity\n partnership management.Aboriginal Youth Cautioning Program\nThe Aboriginal Youth Cautioning Program was introduced as \na police cautioning approach aiming to improve outcomes \nfor Aboriginal young people by diverting them from further \ncontact with the justice system. The program provides \nAboriginal young people in contact with police with an \nopportunity to connect with elders and culturally appropriate \nsupport services in their local area. This connection supports \nyoung people to address the factors underlying their contact \nwith police. Initially trialled in Bendigo, Dandenong and \nEchuca, the program has now been expanded to cover over \n20 Local Government Areas throughout the state.\nFunding received as part of the 2023–24 State Budget \nhas enabled the establishment of five Aboriginal Youth \nCautioning Support Officer (AYCSO) positions. Once \ndeployed, the AYCSOs will support the ongoing expansion \nof the Aboriginal Youth Cautioning Program and will oversee \nthe day-to-day operation of the program, liaising with police, \nAboriginal communities and families to promote better \noutcomes for children and young people. All four police \nregions will receive a dedicated AYCSO position, which will \nbe further supported by one centralised AYCSO overseeing \ncoordination of the program."
        },
        "Police_and_Aboriginal_Community_Protocols": {
            "Main": ""
        },
        "Against_Family_Violence": {
            "Main": "One of the key strategies to address family violence in \nAboriginal communities are the Police and Aboriginal \nCommunity Protocols Against Family Violence. The protocols \nare an agreement between local Aboriginal communities \nand Victoria Police that document the local police response \nto family violence where a person identifies as Aboriginal.\nIn line with the Safe Our Way — Strong Culture, Strong \nPeoples, Strong Families Agreement 20 1 8–2028 , the \nPolice and Aboriginal Community Protocols Against Family \nViolence are being established and renewed state-wide \nacross 1 7 localised partnerships between Aboriginal \ncommunities, Dhelk Dja representatives and Victoria Police. \nDhelk Dja is the key Aboriginal-led Victorian Agreement \nthat commits Aboriginal communities, Aboriginal services, \nand the Victorian Government to work together and be \naccountable for ensuring that Aboriginal people, families, \nand communities are stronger, safer, thriving and living free \nfrom family violence. The protocols provide a mechanism for \ndelivery of localised Aboriginal-led initiatives and actions \nin accordance with the second Dhelk Dja Three Year \nAction Plan 2023–2025 and Action 54 of the Statement \nof Commitment.\n58  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nABORIGINAL-FOCUSED INITIATIVES AND OUTCOMES6Aboriginal Cultural Awareness Training\nAn Aboriginal Cultural Awareness Training (ACAT) package \nwas first delivered to Victoria Police employees in October \n2020. The current training package, endorsed by the \nAboriginal Justice Caucus in May 2022, was developed by a \nregistered Aboriginal training provider and consultant. ACAT \naims to enhance our workforce’s understanding of the rich \nculture and history of Aboriginal Victorians, ensuring a more \nculturally appropriate service response.  \nACAT facilitates organisational engagement in conversations \nabout the true experiences and history of Aboriginal people. \nIt contributes to a culturally safe and inclusive workplace for \nAboriginal employees and enables informed responses to \nAboriginal communities. \nIn March 2023, ACAT was made mandatory for all police \nofficers, PSOs and PCOs. As of 30 June 2024, 1 4,787 staff \nhad completed ACAT."
        },

        "Aboriginal_Complaints_Support_Officer": {
            "Main": "To strengthen the approach to the management of Aboriginal \nidentified complaints, we created a new Aboriginal Complaints \nSupport Officer (ACSO) position and introduced a PALOs \nnetwork into the complaints process to enable a timely and \nculturally appropriate complaint handling experience. The \ncreation of a designated ACSO position within Professional \nStandards Command, acquitted Action 34 of the Statement \nof Commitment. \nFollowing internal and external consultation the ACSO position was finalised and approved by Executive Command in 2023, \nand as of late July 2024 the position has been occupied. \nThe role provides the following benefits:\n keeping complainants updated on progress at key stages of \nthe investigation\n improved communication with Aboriginal complainants\n culturally safe practices across all stages of police complaint \nand oversight investigations\n improved organisational capacity."

About this report\nVictoria Police is the primary law enforcement agency of Victoria, Australia that provides policing services to the community. \nIt was formed in 1 853 and operates under the Victoria Police Act 20 1 3 . Victoria Police is an administrative agency acting \non behalf of the Crown. A description of the nature of its operations and its principal activities is included in the Report of \nOperations, which does not form part of these financial statements.         "Basis_of_preparation": {
            "Main": "These financial statements are prepared in accordance with the historical cost convention except for non-financial physical \nassets and provisions which, subsequent to acquisition, are measured at a revalued amount being their fair value at the date of \nthe revaluation less any subsequent accumulated depreciation and subsequent impairment losses. Refer to Note 8.3.2 Fair Value \nDetermination: Non-Financial Physical Assets.\nThe accrual basis of accounting has been applied in the preparation of these financial statements whereby assets, liabilities, equity, \nincome and expenses are recognised in the reporting period to which they relate, regardless of when cash is received or paid.\nConsistent with the requirements of Change to Australian Accounting Standards Board (AASB) 1004 Contributions , contributions \nby owners (that is, contributed capital and its repayment) are treated as equity transactions and therefore, do not form part of the \nincome and expenses of Victoria Police.\nAdditions to net assets which have been designated as contributions by owners are recognised as contributed capital. \nOther transfers that are in the nature of contributions to or distributions by owners have also been designated as contributions \nby owners.\nJudgements, estimates and assumptions are required to be made about the financial information being presented. Estimates and \nassociated assumptions are based on professional judgements derived from historical experience and various other factors that \nare believed to be reasonable under the circumstances. Actual results may differ from these estimates.\nRevisions to accounting estimates are recognised in the period in which the estimate is revised and also in future periods that \nare affected by the revision. Judgements and assumptions made by management in the application of Australian Accounting \nStandards (AASs) that have significant effects on the financial statements and estimates relate to:\n the fair value of land, buildings, plant and equipment – refer to Note 5. 1 Property, Plant and Equipment and Note 8.3 Fair Value"
        },
        "Determination": {
            "Main": "actuarial assumptions for employee benefit provisions based on likely tenure of existing staff, patterns of leave claims, future salary \nmovements and future discount rates – refer to Note 3. 1.2 Employee Related Provisions\n exercising of lease options under AASB 16 Leases\n Provisions.\nThe primary financial statements show controlled items which generally reflect the capacity of Victoria Police to benefit from that \nitem in the pursuit of its objectives and to deny or regulate the access of others to that benefit. \nAdministered items as disclosed in Note 4 are transactions and activities that are managed or administered by Victoria Police on \nbehalf of the State or another entity. Generally, Victoria Police would lack the capacity to benefit from such transactions in pursuit \nof the State or other entity’s objectives and deny or regulate access of others to that benefit. Administered transactions give rise to \nincome, expenses, assets and liabilities.\nAmounts have been rounded to the nearest thousand dollars unless otherwise stated. The functional and presentation currency of \nVictoria Police is Australian dollars.\n|  73\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7Compliance information\nThese general purpose financial statements have been prepared in accordance with the Financial Management Act 1994  and \napplicable Australian Accounting Standards (AASs) which include Interpretations, issued by the AASB. In particular, they are \npresented in a manner consistent with the requirements of the AASB 1049 Whole of Government and General Government \nSector Financial Reporting . \nWhere appropriate, those AAS paragraphs applicable to not-for-profit entities have been applied.         "Accounting_for_the_goods_and_services_tax": {
            "Main": "Income, expenses and assets are recognised net of the amount of associated Goods and Services Tax (GST), except where \nGST incurred is not recoverable from the Australian Tax Office (ATO). In this case, the GST payable is recognised as part of the \ncost of acquisition of the asset or as part of the expense.\nReceivables and payables are stated inclusive of the amount of GST receivable or payable. The net amount of GST recoverable \nfrom, or payable to, the ATO is included with other receivables or payables in the Balance Sheet.\nCash flows are presented on a gross basis. The GST components of cash flows arising from investing or financing activities which \nare recoverable from, or payable to the ATO are presented as cashflow from operating activities.\nCommitments and contingent liabilities are also stated inclusive of GST.\n74  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS72.         "Income_Recognition_and_Measurement": {
            "Main": "Revenue and income that fund the delivery of Victoria Police’s services are consistently accounted for with the requirements of the \nrelevant accounting standards disclosed in the respective notes in this section. All amounts of income over which Victoria Police \ndoes not have control are disclosed as administered income in Note 4. 1 Administered Items.\n2.2 Income from Transactions\n2.2. 1  Sale of Goods and Services\nThe sale of goods and rendering of services are transactions that Victoria Police has determined to be classified as revenue from \ncontracts with customers in accordance with AASB 1 5. \nPerformance Obligations and Revenue Recognition Policies\nRevenue is measured based on the consideration specified in the contract with the customer. Victoria Police recognises revenue \nwhen it transfers control of a good or service to the customer, i.e. when, or as, the performance obligations for the sale of goods \nand services to the customer are satisfied.\n Customers obtain control of the supplies and consumables at a point in time when the goods are delivered to and have been \naccepted at their premises.\n Revenue from the sale of goods are recognised when the goods are delivered and have been accepted by the customer at \ntheir premises.\n Revenue from the rendering of services is recognised at a point in time when the performance obligation is satisfied when the \nservice is completed; and over time when the customer simultaneously receives and consumes the services as they are provided.\nCustomers are invoiced and revenue is recognised when the goods are delivered and accepted by customers. In rare \ncircumstance where there may be a change in the scope of services provided, the customer will be provided with a new contract \nfor the additional services to be rendered and revenue is recognised consistent with accounting policy above.\n|  75\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7Consideration received in advance of recognising the associated revenue from the customer will be recorded as unearned \nrevenue (contract liability) in Note 6.2 Payables. Where the performance obligations are satisfied but not yet billed, an other \nreceivable (contract asset) is recorded in Note 6. 1 Receivables.\n2.2.2 Other Grants\n($ thousand)\n2024 2023\nGrants Recognised Under AASB 1058 Income of Not-for-Profit Entities  (AASB 1058)\nDonations  - 247\nOther specific purpose grants without any sufficiently specific performance obligations  - 1 1,602\nOther specific purpose grants with sufficiently specific performance obligations 11 ,14 4 2\nGrants Recognised Under AASB 15 Revenue from Contracts with Customers  (AASB 15)\nDonations 19 9\nOther specific purpose grants with sufficiently specific performance obligations 4,504 91\nTotal Grants 15,667 11 , 9 51\nGrants Recognised Under AASB 1058 Income of Not-for-Profit Entities  (AASB 1058)\nVictoria Police has determined that the grant income included in the table above under AASB 1058 has been earned under \narrangements that are either not enforceable and/or linked to sufficiently specific performance obligations.\nIncome from grants without any sufficiently specific performance obligations, or that are not enforceable, is recognised when \nVictoria Police has an unconditional right to receive cash which usually coincides with the receipt of cash. On initial recognition \nof the asset, Victoria Police recognises any related contributions by owners, increases in liabilities, decreases in assets, and \nrevenue (related amounts) in accordance with other Australian Accounting Standards. Related amounts may take the form of:\n contributions by owners, in accordance with AASB 1004 Contributions\n revenue or a contract liability arising from a contract with a customer, in accordance with AASB 1 5\n a lease liability in accordance with AASB 16 Leases\n a financial instrument, in accordance with AASB 9 Financial Instruments , or\n a provision, in accordance with AASB 1 37 Provisions, Contingent Liabilities and Contingent Assets .\nIncome received for specific purpose grants for on-passing is recognised simultaneously as the funds are immediately on-passed \nto the relevant recipient entities on behalf of the Australian Government.\nGrants Recognised Under AASB 15 Revenue from Contracts with Customers  (AASB 15)\nIncome from grants that are enforceable and with sufficiently specific performance obligations are accounted for as revenue \nfrom contracts with customers under AASB 1 5. These grants relate to the funding of various programs/projects. Revenue is \nrecognised when Victoria Police satisfies its performance obligation. This is recognised based on the consideration specified in \nthe funding agreement and to the extent that it is highly probable a significant reversal of the revenue will not occur. The funding \npayments are normally received in advance or shortly after the relevant obligation is satisfied.\n2.2.3 Fair Value of Assets Received Free of Charge or for Nominal Consideration\nContributions of resources received free of charge or for nominal consideration are recognised at fair value when control is \nobtained over them, irrespective of whether these contributions are subject to restrictions or conditions over their use.\nContributions in the form of services are only recognised when a fair value can be reliably determined and the services would \nhave been purchased if not received as a donation.\n2.3 Grants from Victorian Government\n($ thousand)\n2024 2023\nGrants from Victorian Government 4,293,254 4, 1 50,233\nGrants from the Australian Government 1,057  - \nTotal Grants from Victorian Government 4,294,3 1 1 4, 150,233\n76  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7Grants Received from Victorian Government\nIncome from grants received is based on the output Victoria Police provides to the public and is recognised when the output has been \ndelivered and the relevant Minister has certified delivery of the output in accordance with specified performance criteria. Please refer \nto accounting policy in Note 2.2.2 Grants Recognised Under AASB 1058 Income of Not-for-Profit Entities  (AASB 1058).\n2.4 Interest Income\nInterest income  includes interest received on bank term deposits. Interest income is recognised using the effective interest \nmethod which allocates the interest over the relevant period.\n2.5 Other Income\nOther income  relates primarily to boarding fees paid by recruits at the Police Academy and other miscellaneous income earned \nduring the year. Victoria Police recognises the income when it transfers control of a good or service to the customer, that is, when, \nor as, the performance obligations for the sale of goods and services to the customer are satisfied.\n2.6 Annotated Income Agreements\nVictoria Police is permitted under Section 29 of the Financial Management Act to have certain income annotated to the annual \ngrant. The income, which forms part of a Section 29 agreement, is recognised by Victoria Police and the receipts are paid into \nthe Consolidated Fund. At the point of income recognition, Section 29 provides for an equivalent amount to be added to the \nannual grant.         "Wages_and_Salaries_and_Annual_Leave": {
            "Main": "Liabilities for wages, salaries and annual leave are all recognised in the provision for employee benefits as current liabilities \nbecause Victoria Police does not have an unconditional right to defer settlements of these liabilities.\nThe liability for salaries and wages are recognised in the Balance Sheet at remuneration rates which are current at the reporting \ndate. As Victoria Police expects the liabilities to be wholly settled within 1 2 months of  the reporting date, they are measured at \nundiscounted amounts.\nThe annual leave liability is classified as a current liability and measured at the undiscounted amount expected to be paid, as \nVictoria Police does not have an unconditional right to defer settlement of the liability for at least 1 2 months after the end of the \nreporting period.\nNo provision has been made for sick leave as all sick leave is non-vesting and it is not considered probable that the average \nsick leave taken in the future will be greater than the benefits accrued in the future. As sick leave is non-vesting, an expense is \nrecognised in the Comprehensive Operating Statement as it is taken.\nEmployment on-costs such as payroll tax, workers compensation and superannuation are not employee benefits. They are \ndisclosed separately as a component of the provision for employee benefits when the employment to which they relate \nhas occurred.\n|  79\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7Long Service Leave\nLiability for long service leave (LSL) is recognised in the provision for employee benefits.\nUnconditional LSL  is disclosed in the notes to the financial statements as a current liability, even where Victoria Police does \nnot expect to settle the liability within 1 2 months because it will not have the unconditional right to defer the settlement of the \nentitlement should an employee take leave within 1 2 months.\nThe components of this current LSL liability are measured at:\n undiscounted value – if Victoria Police expects to wholly settle within 1 2 months; and\n present value – if Victoria Police does not expect to wholly settle within 1 2 months.\nConditional LSL  is disclosed as a non-current liability. There is an unconditional right to defer the settlement of the entitlement until \nthe employee has completed the requisite years of service. This non-current LSL liability is measured at present value.\nAny gain or loss following the revaluation of the present value of LSL liability is recognised in the “net result from transactions”, \nexcept to the extent that any gain or loss arising from changes in bond interest rates is recognised as an Other Economic Flow in \nthe net result – refer to Note 9. 1 Other Economic Flows Included in Net Result.\nThe Assistant Treasurer approved an alternative wage inflation rate of 5.2 per cent to calculate the LSL liabilities for police officers \nand protective service officers from 20 1 9–24 until the finalisation of the new sworn Enterprise Bargaining Agreement (EBA). \nThe approved inflation rate for Victoria Public Servant (VPS) employees for LSL is 4.450 per cent."
        },
        "Deferred_Salary_Scheme": {
            "Main": "Public service employees may choose to participate in a deferred salary scheme which allows them to be paid the equivalent \nof four years’ salary over a five-year period. During the first four years, the employee receives 80 per cent of their salary. A \nprovision is raised for the balance, which is paid during the fifth year while the employee is on leave. As of 20 1 7 , this scheme is \nonly made available to police officers and protective service officers.\n3. 1.3  Superannuation Contributions\nEmployees of Victoria Police are entitled to receive superannuation benefits and Victoria Police contributes to both defined benefit \nand defined contribution plans. The defined benefit plans provide benefits based on years of service and final average salary.\nAs noted before (in Note 3. 1. 1), the defined benefit liability is recognised by DTF as an administered liability. However, \nsuperannuation contributions paid or payable for the reporting period are included as part of employee benefits in the \nComprehensive Operating Statement of Victoria Police.\nThe names, details and amounts expensed in relation to the major employee superannuation funds and contributions made by \nVictoria Police are as follows:\n($ thousand)\nContributions Paid For The Year Contributions Outstanding At Year End (b)\n2024 2023 2024 2023\nDefined benefit plans (a)\nEmergency Services and State Super 248,708 240,996 664 (55)\nSubtotal 248,708 240,996 664 (55)\nDefined contribution plans:\nVicSuper 23,922 22,290 65 (8)\nOther 34,496 26,6 1 9 83 (7)\nSubtotal 58,418 48,909 1 48 (15)\nTotal 307 , 1 26 289,905 812  (70)\nNotes:\n(a) The basis for determining the level of contributions is determined by the various actuaries of the defined benefit superannuation plans.\n(b) The contributions outstanding at 30 June 2023 represents prepaid balances at year-end.\n80  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS73.2  Operating Expenses\n($ thousand)\n2024 2023"

        "Subsequent_Measurements": {
            "Main": "Non-financial physical assets are measured at fair value (AASB 1 3) on a cyclical basis in accordance with Financial Reporting \nDirection 103 Non-Financial Physical Assets  (FRD 103) issued by the Assistant Treasurer. A full revaluation normally occurs every \nfive years, based upon the government purpose classification of assets, but may occur more frequently if fair value assessments \nindicate material changes in values. Independent valuers are used to conduct these scheduled revaluations and any interim \nrevaluations are determined in accordance with the requirements of the FRDs. \nRevaluation increases or decreases arise from differences between an asset’s carrying value and fair value.\nNet revaluation increases (where the carrying amount of a class of assets is increased as a result of a revaluation) are \nrecognised in “Other economic flows – changes in physical asset revaluation surplus” and accumulated in equity under the \nphysical asset revaluation surplus. However, the net revaluation increase is recognised in the net result to the extent that it reverses \na net revaluation decrease in respect of the same class of property, plant and equipment previously recognised as an expense \n(other economic flows) in the net result. Conversely, net revaluation decreases are recognised immediately as other economic \nflows in the net results unless a credit balance exists in the physical asset revaluation surplus, to which the net revaluation decrease \nis recognised in “Other economic flow – changes in physical asset revaluation surplus” to the extent of the credit balance.\nRevaluation increases and decreases relating to individual assets within a class of property, plant and equipment, are offset \nagainst one another within that class but are not offset in respect of assets in different classes. Any asset revaluation surplus is not \nnormally transferred to accumulated funds on derecognition of the relevant asset.\n90  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7The last independent full revaluation that was undertaken by the Valuer-General Victoria was in 2020–2 1. On a semi-annual \nbasis, Victoria Police monitors the changes in the fair value of each asset and liability through relevant data sources to determine \nwhether any revaluation is required.\nA managerial review of physical land and buildings was undertaken in the current year using indices provided by the  \nValuer-General Victoria. When determining whether a managerial revaluation should occur, the carrying amount of the \nphysical asset class for both Land and Building needs to be considered with the fair valuation on the right-of-use Assets class \nfor Land and Buildings.  Where the movement in the carrying value is greater than the 10 per cent threshold under FRD 103 \nNon-Financial Physical Assets  a managerial revaluation can occur. In both Asset classes the movement was below the  \n10 per cent threshold.\n|  91\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS76.         "Aviation": {
            "Main": "The aviation fleet has been custom-built and specialised in nature for Victoria Police. The Leonardo AW1 39 Helicopters are \nfitted with high-definition cameras and the most advanced mapping and infrared camera technology. They can reach speeds of \n250 kilometers per hour and fly for 1000 kilometres without needing to refuel. Its highest and best use is proven by the utilisation of \nthe monthly flying hours, averaging 94 per cent of the flying hours regulatory limit. The aviation fleet undergoes regular maintenance \nand upgrades to ensure the fleet are functional and operational ready and adhere to strict regulations under the Civil Aviation \nSafety Authority. Victoria Police has considered the carrying amount to approximate the fair value at 30 June 2024\nNon-specialised land, non-specialised buildings and cultural artworks \nNon-specialised land, non-specialised buildings and cultural artworks are valued using the market approach, whereby assets \nare compared to recent comparable sales or sales of comparable assets that are considered to have nominal value."
        },
        "Specialised_land_and_specialised_buildings": {
            "Main": "The market approach is also used for specialised land, although it is adjusted for the Community Service Obligation (CSO) to \nreflect the specialised nature of the land being valued.\nThe CSO adjustment is a reflection of the valuer’s assessment of the impact of restrictions associated with an asset to the extent \nthat the CSO adjustment is also equally applicable to market participants.\nFor the majority of Victoria Police’s specialised buildings, the current replacement cost method is used, adjusting for the \nassociated depreciation."
        },
        "Heritage_buildings": {
            "Main": "Heritage buildings are valued using the current replacement cost method. This cost generally represents the replacement cost of \nthe building/component after applying depreciation rates on a useful life basis. However, for some heritage and iconic assets, \nthe cost may be the reproduction cost rather than the replacement cost if those assets’ service potential could only be replaced \nby reproducing them with the same materials.\n86  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7Vehicles \nVehicles are valued using the current replacement cost method. Victoria Police acquires new vehicles and at times disposes of \nthem before the end of their economic life. The process of acquisition, use and disposal in the market is managed by experienced \nfleet managers in Victoria Police who set relevant depreciation rates during use to reflect the utilisation of the vehicles.\nFair value for plant and equipment  that are specialised in use is determined using the current replacement cost method.\nRefer to Note 8.3 Fair Value Determination for additional information on fair value determination of property, plant and equipment.\nImpairment of Property, Plant and Equipment\nThe recoverable amount of primarily non-cash-generating assets of not-for-profit entities, which are typically specialised in \nnature and held for continuing use of their service capacity, is expected to be materially the same as fair value determined under \nAASB 1 3 Fair Value Measurement , with the consequence that AASB 1 36 Impairment of Assets  does not apply to such assets that \nare regularly revalued.\n5. 1. 1  Depreciation and Amortisation\n($ thousand)\nNotes 2024 2023\nLand (right-of-use assets) 644 644 \nDepreciation of buildings (including heritage buildings and right-of-use assets) 1 43,66 1 141,923  \nDepreciation of plant and equipment 90,659 87 ,344 \nAmortisation of intangible assets 5.2 30,460 30,658 \nTotal Depreciation and Amortisation 265,424 260,569 \nAll buildings (including leasehold improvements), plant and equipment and other non-financial physical assets that have finite useful \nlives are depreciated.         "Initial_Recognition": {
            "Main": "Purchased intangible assets are initially measured at cost. When the recognition criteria in AASB 1 38 Intangible Assets  are met, \ninternally generated assets are recognised at cost. Subsequently, intangible assets with finite useful lives are carried at cost less \naccumulated amortisation and accumulated impairment losses. Amortisation begins when the asset is available for use, that is, \nwhen it is in the location and condition necessary for it to be capable of operating in the manner intended by management. \nCosts incurred subsequent to initial acquisition are capitalised when it is expected that additional future economic benefits will \nflow to Victoria Police.\nAn internally generated intangible asset arising from development (or from the development phase of an internal project) is \nrecognised if, and only if, all of the following are demonstrated:\n(a) the technical feasibility of completing the intangible asset so that it will be available for use or sale;\n(b) an intention to complete the intangible asset for use or sale;\n(c) the ability to use or sell the intangible asset;\n(d) the intangible asset will generate probable future economic benefits;\n(e) the availability of adequate technical, financial and other resources to complete the development and to use or sell the \nintangible asset; and\n(f) the ability to measure reliably the expenditure attributable to the intangible asset during its development."
        },
        "Subsequent_Measurement": {
            "Main": "Intangible produced assets with finite useful lives, are amortised as an “expense from transactions” on a straight-line basis over \ntheir useful lives. Produced intangible assets have useful lives of between three and 1 2 years.\nIntangible non-produced assets with finite lives are amortised as an “other economic flow” on a straight-line basis over their \nuseful lives.             "Main": "Contractual receivables are classified as financial instruments and categorised as financial assets at amortised costs, with \nthe exception of derivative financial instruments which are categorised as fair value through profit and loss.         "Statutory_receivables": {
            "Main": "Statutory receivables do not arise from contracts and are recognised and measured similarly to contractual receivables (except \nfor impairment) but are not classified as financial instruments for disclosure purposes. Victoria Police applies AASB 9 Financial \nInstruments  for initial measurement of the statutory receivables and as a result statutory receivables are initially recognised at fair \nvalue plus any directly attributable transaction cost. Amounts recognised from the Victorian Government represent funding for all \ncommitments incurred and are drawn from the Consolidated Fund as the commitments fall due.\nDetails about Victoria Police’s impairment policies, exposure to credit risk and the calculation of the loss allowance are set out in \nNote 8. 1.2 Financial Risk Management Objectives and Policies."

The lease contracts are typically made for fixed \nperiods of 1-30 years with an option to renew the lease after that date. Depending on the lease contract terms, property lease \npayments are usually re-negotiated every five years to reflect the market rentals.\n7 .2. 1 (a) Short-Term Lease (Lease term of 1 2 months or less)\nVictoria Police has elected to account for short-term leases using the practical expedients. Instead of recognising a right-of-use \nasset and lease liability, the payments in relation to these are recognised as an expense in profit or loss on a straight-line basis \nover the lease term. As of 30 June 2024, Victoria Police has committed to six short-term leases and the total commitment at that \ndate is $0. 1 3 million (2023: $0.02 million).\n7 .2. 1 (b) Low Value Leases \nLow value leases with the underlying asset’s fair value (when new, regardless of the age of the asset being leased) is no more \nthan $10,000. Payments on low value leases are recognised on a straight-line basis.\n7 .2. 1 (c) Right-of-Use Assets\nRight-of-use assets are presented in Note 5. 1(a).\n7 .2. 1 (d) Amounts Recognised in the Comprehensive Operating Statement\nThe following amounts are recognised in the Comprehensive Operating Statement relating to leases:\n($ thousand)\n2024 2023\nInterest expense on lease liabilities 58,797 59,857 \nExpenses relating to short-term leases 516 318  \nIncome from sub-leasing (5, 160) (4,84 1)\nTotal amount recognised in the Comprehensive Operating Statement 54, 153 55,334 \n7 .2. 1 (e) Amounts Recognised in the Cashflow Statement\nThe following amounts are recognised in the Statement of Cashflows for the year-ended 30 June 2024 relating to leases:\n($ thousand)\n2024 2023\nTotal cash outflow for leases 86,632 103,260 \nFor any new contracts entered into, Victoria Police considers whether a contract is, or contains a lease. A lease is defined as \n“a contract, or part of a contract, that conveys the right to use an asset (the underlying asset) for a period of time in exchange \nfor consideration”. To apply this definition, Victoria Police assesses whether the contract meets three key evaluations which are \nwhether:\n the contract contains an identified asset, which is either explicitly identified in the contract or implicitly specified by being \nidentified at the time the asset is made available to Victoria Police and for which the supplier does not have substantive \nsubstitution rights\n Victoria Police has the right to obtain substantially all of the economic benefits from use of the identified asset throughout the \nperiod of use, considering its rights within the defined scope of the contract and Victoria Police has the right to direct the use of \nthe identified asset throughout the period of use\n Victoria Police has the right to make decisions in respect of “how and for what purpose” the asset is used throughout the \nperiod of use.\n96  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7Separation of Lease and Non-Lease components\nAt inception or on reassessment of a contract that contains a lease component, the lessee is required to separate out and \naccount separately for non-lease components within a lease contract and exclude these amounts when determining the lease \nliability and right-of-use asset amount."

        "Recognition_and_Measurement_of_Leases_as_a_Lessee": {
            "Main": "Lease Liability – Initial Measurement\nThe lease liability is initially measured at the present value of the lease payments unpaid at the commencement date, discounted \nusing the interest rate implicit in the lease if that rate is readily determinable or the incremental borrowing rate as provided by DTF.\nLease payments included in the measurement of the lease liability comprise the following:\n fixed payments (including in-substance fixed payments) less any lease incentive receivable\n variable payments based on an index or rate, initially measured using the index or rate as at the commencement date\n amounts expected to be payable under a residual value guarantee\n payments arising from purchase and termination options reasonably certain to be exercised.\nLease Liability – Subsequent Measurement\nSubsequent to initial measurement, the liability will be reduced for payments made and increased for interest. It is remeasured to \nreflect any reassessment or modification, or if there are changes in-substance fixed payments.\nWhen the lease liability is remeasured, the corresponding adjustment is reflected in the right-of-use asset, or profit and loss if the \nright-of-use asset is already reduced to zero.\nShort-Term Leases \nPlease refer to Note 7 .2. 1 (a) Short Term Lease (Lease term of 1 2 months or less)."

            "Main": "Please refer to Note 7 .2. 1 (b) Low Value Leases.\nBelow Market/Peppercorn Leases\nRight-of-use assets under leases at significantly below-market terms and conditions that are entered into principally to the \norganisation to further its objectives, are initially and subsequently measured at cost. As of 30 June 2024, Victoria Police has no \nbelow market/peppercorn leases.\nPresentation of Right-of-Use Assets and Lease Liabilities\nVictoria Police presents right-of-use assets as “property plant equipment” unless they meet the definition of investment property, in \nwhich case they are disclosed as “investment property” in the Balance Sheet. Lease liabilities are presented as “borrowings” in \nthe Balance Sheet.\n7 .2.2 Sub-Leasing in 3 1 1 Spencer Street\nVictoria Police has sub-leased approximately 1 3 per cent of its whole office space to third parties. These sub-leases are \nnegotiated on an individual basis and contain a wide range of different terms and conditions (including termination and renewal \nrights). The floors are rented for between 3 –1 5 years with options to renew the contract. The lease payments are fixed and \nadjusted annually for inflation.\nVictoria Police is a sub-lessor (intermediate lessor) of the right-of-use assets.\nAn intermediate lessor classifies the sub-lease as either a finance or operating lease. A lease is classified as a finance lease if it \ntransfers substantially all the risks and rewards from the right-of-use assets resulting from the head lease; otherwise, it is classified \nas an operating lease.\nAs all the risks and rewards are not substantially transferred to the lessee, Victoria Police has classified the sub-lease as an \noperating lease. Lease income from these operating leases is recognised on a straight-line basis over the lease term.\n|  97\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS77 .3  Cash Flow Information and Balances\nCash and deposits, including cash equivalents, comprise cash on hand and cash at bank, deposits at call and those highly \nliquid investments with an original maturity of three months or less, which are held for the purpose of meeting short term cash \ncommitments rather than for investment purposes, and are readily convertible to known amounts of cash and are subject to an \ninsignificant risk of changes in value.\n($ thousand)\n2024 2023\nCash and term deposits (a)472 1, 1 96 \nFunds held in trust (b)73,2 16 69,6 1 7 \nBalance as per Cash Flow Statement 73,688 70,813 \nNotes:\n(a) The term deposits held during the year were bearing a weighted average interest rate of 4.20 per cent (2023: 2.87 per cent). \n(b) These are monies provided by outside organisations for special purpose projects related to the normal operating activities of  Victoria Police. The funds are held in a suspense account at the DTF on behalf of Victoria Police. \nDue to the state’s investment policy and funding arrangements, Victoria Police does not hold a large cash reserve in its bank \naccounts. Cash received from generation of income is generally paid into the State’s bank account (public account). Similarly, \nexpenditure, including in the form of cheques drawn for payments to Victoria Police’s suppliers and creditors are made via the \npublic account. The public account remits to Victoria Police the cash required upon presentation of cheques by Victoria Police’s \nsuppliers or creditors.\nThese funding arrangements often results in Victoria Police having a notional shortfall in the cash at bank required for payment of \nunpresented cheques at the reporting date.             "Main": "Financial assets are measured at amortised costs if both of the following criteria are met and the assets are not designated as fair \nvalue through net result:\n the assets are held by Victoria Police to collect the contractual cash flows\n the assets’ contractual terms give rise to cash flows that are solely payments of principal and interest.\nThese assets are initially recognised at fair value plus any directly attributable transaction costs and are subsequently measured \nat amortised cost using the effective interest method less any impairment.\nVictoria Police recognises the following assets in this category:\n cash and deposits (which includes term deposits) – refer to Note 7 .3 Cash Flow Information and Balances\n receivables (excluding statutory receivables and derivative financial instruments) – refer to Note 6. 1 Receivables."

        "Categories_of_financial_liabilities": {
            "Main": "Financial liabilities at amortised cost  are initially recognised on the date they are originated. They are initially measured at fair \nvalue plus any directly attributable transaction costs. Subsequent to initial recognition, these financial instruments are measured \nat amortised cost with any difference between the initial recognised amount and the redemption value being recognised in the \nComprehensive Operating Statement over the period of the interest-bearing liability, using the effective interest rate method. \nVictoria Police recognises the following liabilities in this category: \n payables (excluding statutory payables and derivative financial instruments) – refer to Note 6.2 Payables\n borrowings (including lease liabilities) – refer to Note 7 . 1 Borrowings.\nDerivative financial instruments  are transacted for hedging purposes and are initially recognised at fair value on the date on \nwhich a derivative contract is entered into. Derivatives are carried as assets when their fair value is positive and as liabilities when \ntheir fair value is negative. Where derivative financial instruments have been designated into hedge accounting relationships, \nthe effective gains or losses arising from changes in the fair value of derivatives after initial recognition are recognised in other \ncomprehensive income.\nDerecognition of financial liabilities:  A financial liability is derecognised when the obligation under the liability is discharged, \ncancelled or expires.\nWhen an existing financial liability is replaced by another from the same lender on substantially different terms, or the terms of an \nexisting liability are substantially modified, the exchange or modification is treated as a derecognition of the original liability and \nthe recognition of a new liability. The difference in the respective carrying amounts is recognised as an “Other Economic Flow” in \nthe Comprehensive Operating Statement.\n102  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7Reclassification of financial instruments:  Subsequent to initial recognition, reclassification of financial liabilities is not permitted. \nFinancial assets are required to be reclassified between fair value through net results, fair value through Other Comprehensive \nIncome and amortised cost when and only when Victoria Police’s business model for managing financial assets has changed \nsuch that its previous model would no longer apply.\nVictoria Police is generally unable to change its business model because it is determined by the Performance Management \nFramework and it is required to apply the framework under the Standing Directions under the Financial Management Act.\n8. 1. 1 Financial Instruments: Categorisation\n($ thousand)\n2024 NotesCash and \ndepositsDerivatives \ndesignated \nin hedge \nrelationship Contractual \nfinancial assets \nat amortised \ncostContractual \nfinancial \nliabilities at \namortised cost Total\nContractual financial assets (a)\nCash and deposits 7. 3 73,688 - - - 73,688\nReceivables (b)\nSale of goods and services 6 .1 - - 4, 1 28 - 4, 1 28\nOther receivables 6 .1 - - 8,268 - 8,268\nDerivative financial instruments 6 .1 - 374 - - 374\nTotal contractual financial assets 73,688 374 1 2,396 - 86,458\nContractual financial liabilities (a)\nPayables (b)\nSupplies and services 6.2 - - - 12 2 , 7 78 12 2 , 7 78\nAmounts payable to government and agencies 6.2 - - - 439 439\nOther payables 6.2 - - - 7, 872 7, 872\nDerivative financial instruments 6.2 - 3,185 - - 3,185"

        "Statutory_receivables_at_amortised_cost": {
            "Main": "Victoria Police’s non-contractual receivables arising from statutory requirements are not financial instruments. However, they \nare nevertheless recognised and measured in accordance with AASB 9 requirements as if those receivables are financial \ninstruments.\nThe statutory receivables are considered to have low credit risk, taking into account the counterparty’s credit rating, risk of default \nand capacity to meet contractual cash flow obligations in the near term. As the result, the loss allowance recognised for these \nfinancial assets during the period was limited to 1 2 months of expected losses. No loss allowance was recognised.\n|  105\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7Financial Instruments: Liquidity Risk\nLiquidity risk arises from Victoria Police being unable to meet its financial obligations as they fall due. Victoria Police operates \nunder the Government fair payments policy of settling financial obligations within 10 business days with a contract value of less \nthan $3 million and in the event of a dispute, making payments within 30 days from the date of resolution.\nVictoria Police is exposed to liquidity risk mainly through the financial liabilities as disclosed in the face of the Balance Sheet and \nthe amounts related to financial guarantees. Victoria Police manages its liquidity risk by:\n close monitoring of its short-term and long-term borrowings by senior management, including monthly reviews on current and \nfuture borrowing levels and requirements\n maintaining an adequate level of uncommitted funds that can be drawn at short notice to meet its short-term obligations\n careful maturity planning of its financial obligations based on forecasts of future cash flows\n a high credit rating for the State of Victoria (Moody’s Investor Services and Standard & Poor’s double-A, which assists in \naccessing debt market at a lower interest rate).\nVictoria Police’s exposure to liquidity risk is deemed insignificant based on prior period’s data and current assessment of risk. \nCash for unexpected events is generally sourced from making a request to the Treasury Corporation of Victoria. The carrying \namount detailed in the following table of contractual financial liabilities recorded in the financial statements represents Victoria \nPolice’s maximum exposure to liquidity risk. \nMaturity Analysis of Contractual Financial Liabilities (a)\n($ thousand)"

Victoria Police does not hold any interest-bearing financial instruments that are measured at fair value, and therefore has \nno exposure to fair value interest rate risk."

        "Interest_Rate_Sensitivity_Analysis": {
            "Main": "Victoria Police interest rate risk sensitivity is insignificant with the cash and deposit balance for the current year $4.3 million \n(2023: $3.5 million). \nVictoria Police manages this risk by mainly undertaking fixed rate or non-interest bearing financial instruments with relatively \neven maturity profiles, with only insignificant amounts of financial instruments at floating rate.         "Foreign_Currency_Risk": {
            "Main": "All foreign currency transactions during the financial year are brought to account using the exchange rate in effect at the date \nof the transaction. Foreign monetary items existing at the end of the reporting period are translated at the closing rate at the \ndate of the end of the reporting period. There are no non-monetary assets carried at fair value that are denominated in foreign \ncurrencies.\nVictoria Police is exposed to foreign currency risk mainly through payables relating to purchases of supplies and consumables \nfrom overseas as well as leasing of aviation aircrafts, building Police Patrol and Rescue Water Vessel, and related services. For \nthe purchase of supplies and consumables from overseas, there are only limited amounts of transactions denominated in foreign \ncurrencies and there is a relatively short timeframe between commitment and settlement, therefore risk is minimal and immaterial. \nIn regard to the Rotary Wing Aircraft Supply and Support Agreement, Victoria Police has entered into forward foreign exchange \noptions, to hedge foreign currency risk exposure associated with payments in United States dollars estimated based on the \nestimated flying hours. For the cost of the flying hours, 95 per cent is hedged of which 80 per cent is through forward exchange \ncontracts and 1 5 per cent is through forward exchange options. The derivative contracts are for a term of 10 years ending on 30 \nAugust 2030.\nOn 30 June 2023, Victoria Police entered into forward exchange contracts to hedge foreign currency risk exposure for the \nbuilding of a Police Patrol and Rescue Water Vessel, which associated with payments in New Zealand dollars based on the cost \nof the project less the initial payment made on 30 June 2023. The derivative contracts are for a term of 2.5 years with the final \nmilestone to be met on 2 1 December 2025."

        "Contingent_assets": {
            "Main": "Contingent assets are possible assets that arise from past events, whose existence will be confirmed only by the occurrence or \nnon-occurrence of one or more uncertain future events not wholly within the control of the entity.\nThese are classified as either quantifiable, where the potential economic benefit is known, or non-quantifiable.\nNo contingent assets have been recognised for the year ended 30 June 2024 (2023: Nil)."
        },
        "Contingent_liabilities": {
            "Main": "Contingent liabilities are:\n possible obligations that arise from past events, whose existence will be confirmed only by the occurrence or non-occurrence \nof one or more uncertain more uncertain future events not wholly within the control of the entity, or\n present obligations that arise from past events but are not recognised because:\n »it is not probable that an outflow of resources embodying economic benefits will be required to settle the obligations, or\n »the amount of the obligation cannot be measured with sufficient reliability.\nContingent liabilities are classified as either quantifiable or non-quantifiable.\nNon-quantifiable contingent liabilities\nNon-quantifiable contingent liabilities include potential obligations arising from indemnities provided in relation to transactions \n(including financial arrangements and consultancy services) as well as for directors and administrators, performance guarantees, \nwarranties and letters of comfort, deeds in respect of certain obligations and unclaimed monies, which may be subject to future \nclaims by the general public against the State. \nSince the conclusion of the Royal Commission into the Management of Police Informants (RCMPI), the State of Victoria (Victoria \nPolice) has been served with a small number of civil claims. The outcomes of these civil claims and a number of Court of Appeal \ncriminal matters, as well as ongoing disclosure work by Victoria Police, will likely dictate whether further claims are received. \nGiven those circumstances, it is not possible to reliably quantify any contingent liabilities relating to potential matters arising from \nthe conduct explored by the RCMPI."
        },

Fair value is \nthe price that would be received from selling an asset or paid to transfer a liability in an orderly transaction between market \nparticipants at the measurement date.\nThe following assets and liabilities are carried at fair value: \n financial assets and liabilities at fair value through the Comprehensive Operating Statement;\n land, buildings, infrastructure, plant and equipment, right-of-use assets.\nIn addition, the fair values of other assets and liabilities that are carried at amortised cost, also need to be determined for \ndisclosure purposes. \nVictoria Police determines the policies and procedures for determining fair values for both financial and non-financial assets and \nliabilities as required."
        },
        "Fair_Value_Hierarchy": {
            "Main": "In determining fair values, a number of inputs is used. To increase consistency and comparability in the financial statements, these \ninputs are categorised into three levels, also known as the fair value hierarchy. The levels are as follows:\nLevel 1 – quoted (unadjusted) market prices in active markets for identical assets or liabilities\nLevel 2 – valuation techniques for which the lowest level input that is significant to the fair value measurement is directly or \nindirectly observable\nLevel 3 – valuation techniques for which the lowest level input that is significant to the fair value measurement is unobservable.\nVictoria Police determines whether transfers have occurred between levels in the hierarchy by reassessing categorisation (based \non the lowest level input that is significant to the fair value measurement as a whole) at the end of each reporting period.\nThe Valuer -General Victoria is Victoria Police’s independent valuation agency. Victoria Police, in conjunction with the Valuer-\nGeneral Victoria, monitors changes in the fair value of each asset and liability through relevant data sources to determine \nwhether revaluation is required.\nFor those assets and liabilities for which fair values are determined, the following disclosures are provided:\n carrying amount and the fair value (which would be the same for those assets measured at fair value)\n which level of the fair value hierarchy was used to determine the fair value, and\n in respect of those assets and liabilities subject to fair value determination using Level 3 inputs:\n »a reconciliation of the movements in fair values from the beginning of the year to the end, and\n »details of significant unobservable inputs used in the fair value determination.\nThis section is divided between disclosures in connection with fair value determination for financial instruments (refer to Note \n8.3. 1 Fair Value Determination of Financial Assets and Liabilities) and non-financial physical assets (refer to Note 8.3.2 Fair Value \nDetermination: Non-Financial Physical Assets).\n8.3. 1  Fair Value Determination of Financial Assets and Liabilities\nThe fair value of financial assets and liabilities is determined as follows:\nLevel 1 – The fair value of financial instruments with standard terms and conditions and traded in active liquid markets is \ndetermined with reference to quoted market prices\nLevel 2 – The fair value is determined using inputs other than quoted prices that are observable for the financial asset or liability, \neither directly or indirectly, and\nLevel 3 – The fair value is determined in accordance with generally accepted pricing models based on discounted cash flow \nanalysis using unobservable market inputs.\nVictoria Police currently holds Level 1 and 2 financial instruments. We consider the carrying amount of financial assets and \nfinancial liabilities recorded in the financial report to be a fair approximation of their fair value, because of the short-term nature \nof the financial instruments and the expectation that they will be paid in full by the end of the 2024–25 reporting period, with the \nexception of derivative financial instruments and borrowings which are determined as Level 2.\n|  109\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7These financial instruments include:"

        "Cultural_Assets_at_Fair_Value": {
            "Main": "Artworks 5,239  -  - 5,239 \nTotal of Cultural Assets at Fair Value 5,239  -  - 5,239 \nNotes:\n(a) Classified in accordance with the fair value hierarchy.\n(b) Non-specialised land and buildings are residential properties used by police officers and protective service officers.\n(c) These heritage assets cannot be modified nor disposed of without formal ministerial approval.\n(d) This amount forms part of the balance for Buildings at Fair Value (excluding heritage buildings) in Note 5.1 Property, Plant and Equipment.\nThere have been no transfers between levels during the year.\nNon-specialised land and non-specialised buildings  are valued using the market approach and current replacement cost whereby \nassets are compared to recent comparable sales or sales of comparable assets that are considered to have nominal value. \nEvery 5 years, an independent valuation of land and buildings is performed by the Valuer-General Victoria to determine the fair \nvalue using the market approach. The last independent valuation was performed during the 2020–2 1 financial year. The fair \nvalue of assets was determined by analysing sales and allowing for share, size, topography, location and other relevant factors \nspecific to the asset being valued. From this analysis, an appropriate rate per square metre has been applied to the subject \nasset. During the intervening periods, fair value assessment using the indices provided the Valuer-General Victoria is performed \non a semi-annual basis as required by FRD 103 Non-Financial Physical Assets to determine that the carrying values of land and \nbuilding approximate fair values. \nTo the extent that non-specialised land and non-specialised buildings do not contain significant, unobservable adjustments, these \nassets are classified as Level 2 under the market approach.\nSpecialised land and specialised buildings:  The market approach is also used for specialised land, although is adjusted for the \nCommunity Service Obligation (CSO) to reflect the specialised nature of the land being valued. \nThe CSO adjustment is a reflection of the valuer’s assessment of the impact of restrictions associated with an asset to the extent \nthat is also equally applicable to market participants. This approach is in light of the highest and best use consideration required \nfor fair value measurement and takes into account the use of the asset that is physically possible, legally permissible, and \nfinancially feasible. As adjustments of CSO are considered significant unobservable inputs, specialised land would be classified \nas Level 3 assets. A significant increase or decrease in the CSO adjustment would result in a significantly lower (higher) fair value.\nFor the majority of Victoria Police’s specialised buildings, the current replacement cost method is used, adjusting for the associated \ndepreciations. As depreciation adjustments are considered significant, unobservable inputs, specialised buildings are classified \nas Level 3 fair value measurements. \n|  111\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS7The last independent valuation of Victoria Police’s specialised land and specialised buildings was performed by the  \nValuer-General Victoria as at 30 June 202 1. \nHeritage assets  are valued using the current replacement cost method. This cost generally represents the replacement cost of \nthe building/component after applying depreciation rates on a useful life basis. However, for some heritage and iconic assets, \nthe cost may be the reproduction cost rather than the current replacement cost if those assets’ service potential could only be \nreplaced by reproducing them with the same materials.\nWhere it has not been possible to examine hidden works such as structural frames and floors, the use of reasonable materials \nand methods of construction have been assumed bearing in mind the age and nature of the building. The estimated cost of \nreconstruction including structure services and finishes, also factors in any heritage classifications as applicable.\nAn independent valuation of Victoria Police’s heritage assets was performed by the Valuer-General Victoria. The valuation \nwas performed based on the current replacement cost of the assets. The effective date of the valuation was 30 June 202 1. As \nadjustments of heritage assets are considered significant unobservable inputs, these assets would be classified as Level 3 assets."

No provision has been required, nor any expense recognised, for \nimpairment of receivables from related parties.\n9.5 Subsequent Events\nSubsequent to the reporting period, one non-adjusting event occurred after the reporting period.\nVictorian Public Service Enterprise Agreement 2024\nA non-adjusting subsequent event occurred where on 5 July 2024, Victorian Public Sector (VPS) employees voted to accept the \nproposed VPS Enterprise Bargaining Agreement (EBA). Industrial Relations Victoria and the Community and Public Sector Union \n(CPSU) submitted the VPS EBA to the Fair Work Commission where it was formally approved on 1 2 August 2024. \nThe first salary and allowance increase apply from 1 May 2024. These increases, as well as other entitlements, will occur after \nthe Agreement receives formal approval from the Fair Work Commission and will be recognised in the financial year 2024–25.\nThe estimated financial effect on the reporting period (1 May 2024 to 30 June 2024) is $2.5 million or 0.5 per cent of total \nSalaries and allowances impacting 4,328 VPS employees.\n|  119\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\nFINANCIAL PERFORMANCE AND FINANCIAL STATEMENTS79.6 Other Accounting Policies"

        "Contributions_by_Owners": {
            "Main": "Consistent with the requirements of AASB 1004 Contributions , contributions by the owner (that is, contributed capital and its \nrepayment) are treated as equity transactions and therefore, do not form part of the income and expenses of Victoria Police.\nAdditions to net assets which have been designated as contributions by owners are recognised as contributed capital. Other \ntransfers that are in the nature of contributions or distributions have also been designated as contributions by owners.\nTransfers of net assets arising from administrative restructurings are treated as distributions to or contributions by owners. Transfers \nof net liabilities arising from administrative restructurings are treated as distributions to owners.\n9.7  Australian Accounting Standards Issued That Are Not Yet Effective\nCertain new and revised accounting standards have been issued but are not effective for the 2023–24 reporting period. These \naccounting standards have not been applied to the Financial Statements. \nAASB 2022-10 Amendments to Australian Accounting Standards – Fair Value Measurement of Non-Financial \nAssets of Not-for-Profit Public Sector Entities.\nAASB 2022-10 amends AASB 1 3 Fair Value Measurement  by adding authoritative implementation guidance and illustrative \nexamples for fair value measurement of non-financial assets of not-for-profit sector entitles not held primarily for their ability to \ngenerate net cash inflows.\nAmong other things, the Standard:\n specifies that an entity needs to consider whether an asset’s highest and best use differs from its current use only when it is held \nfor sale or held for distribution to owners under AASB 5 Non-current Assets Held for Sale and Discontinued Operations  or if it \nis highly probable that it will be used for an alternative purpose\n clarifies that an asset’s use is “financially feasible” if market participants would be willing to invest in the asset’s service capacity, \nconsidering both the capacity to provide needed goods or services and the resulting costs of those goods and services\n specifies that if both market selling price and some market participant data required to fair value the asset are not observable, \nan entity needs to start with its own assumptions and adjust them to the extent that reasonably available information indicates \nthat other market participants would use different data\n provide guidance on the application of the cost approach to fair value, including the nature of costs to be included in a \nreference asset and identificaiton of economic obsolescence. \nThis Standard applies prospectively to annual periods beginning on or after 1 January 2024, with earlier application permitted. \nSeveral other amending standards and AASB interpretations have been issued that apply to future reporting periods, but are \nconsidered to have limited impact on Victoria Police’s reporting.\n9.8  Style Conventions\nFigures in the tables and in the text have been rounded to the nearest thousand dollars, unless otherwise stated. Discrepancies in \ntables between totals and sums of components reflect rounding. Percentage variations in all tables are based on the underlying \nunrounded amounts.\nThe notation used in the tables is as follows:\n– zero or rounded to zero\n(xxx.x) negative numbers\n200x year period.\n120  |\nANNUAL REPORT 2023–24\n\n----------PAGE BREAK----------\n\n8 APPENDICES\nAppendix A: Disclosure index"
